Key Shift

COLLEAGUE.SKILL — distilling a person's judgment into an editable, versioned skill file

AlphaSignal AI (@AlphaSignalAI)

Shanghai AI Lab's COLLEAGUE.SKILL (the paper behind the viral 'dot-skill' tool) was the #1 Paper of the Day on Hugging Face. It distills a specific coworker into an agent skill by splitting capability (work.md) from behavior/persona (persona.md), pulling from chat history and docs. Technical report released June 1, 2026; community gallery already at 215 skills / 165 contributors. Reframes expertise as editable, versioned files — not an opaque clone. Distinct from SkillOpt (#95), which auto-optimizes a generic skill against a validation set; this captures one named person's tacit judgment.

Worth Your Time?

READ. One-line verdict: the clearest sign yet that the unit of "knowledge work capital" is shifting from the person to a versioned file of the person's judgment — and Every Consulting already sells the manual version of this.

What's Actually Happening

The viral framing ("distill your departing coworker into an AI") is the marketing. The structural move underneath is the interesting part: COLLEAGUE.SKILL formalizes tacit expertise as two separable artifacts — work.md (the capability: what the person does, their decision rules, their checklists) and persona.md (the behavior: voice, taste, what they'd push back on, their quirks). It ingests the raw exhaust of a knowledge worker's job — chat history (Lark/DingTalk), documents, work communications — and compiles it into a manual an agent can execute.

Why now: agents got good enough at following a detailed skill file that the bottleneck moved upstream, to authoring one. Hand-writing a good skill is hard and depends on the author's prompting ability. COLLEAGUE.SKILL automates the authoring by pointing it at a person's actual work history. The 24-year-old engineer who built it in four hours, the 215-skill gallery, the #1 HuggingFace slot — that's adoption velocity, and velocity is the signal, not the polish.

Who benefits from the narrative: the platform/tool layer (more skills = more lock-in) and management (institutional knowledge stops walking out the door). Who loses control of the narrative: the individual contributor, whose leverage has always partly been that the knowledge lived in their head.

What's Not Being Said

The viral-tweet framing is upbeat ("save yourself from repetitive work"). The honest version is darker and is being reported elsewhere: in China this tool set off real anxiety — workers realizing they were being asked to train their own replacements, and pushing back (MIT Technology Review, Apr 2026; SCMP). The contrarian read: "expertise capture" and "expertise extraction" are the same operation viewed from two ends of the power asymmetry. Whether it's empowering or extractive depends entirely on who commissions the skill file and who owns it afterward.

The second thing not being said: a skill file is a lossy compression of judgment. It captures the decisions that left a paper trail, not the instinct that decided which decisions mattered. The first generation of these will be confidently wrong in exactly the high-stakes edge cases where the real expert's value lived — and you won't know until the expert is gone. The editable-and-versioned design is the honest answer to this: it's a living document you correct, not a frozen clone you trust.

Where This Sits in the Bigger Picture

This is the same structural force as the Levie / Kirkland-builds-its-own-Harvey thesis bookmarked the same day, and the Single Company Brain theme: when everyone has the same frontier models, the moat is internal institutional knowledge. COLLEAGUE.SKILL is what that thesis looks like at the level of one person instead of one firm. It sits squarely in AI / labor-market transformation — not "AI takes the job" but the subtler "AI changes what the job's durable asset is." The asset is no longer your presence; it's the captured, versioned file of your judgment, and the question of who owns that file is the new labor-rights frontier.

It also rhymes with Claudie's own architecture: her identity.md (persona) is split from her skills (capability) — the exact persona.md / work.md decomposition, arrived at independently. That convergence is itself signal: the split isn't arbitrary, it's the natural shape of "an agent that acts like a specific competent person."

What's Likely Coming Next
  • Precedent: every time tacit knowledge got codified — guild secrets → trade manuals, craftsman → assembly-line work instructions, senior dev → runbook — the codification raised throughput and lowered the codified worker's individual bargaining power while creating a new, higher tier of work (judging/editing the codified output). Expect the same: a new tier of "skill-file editors/validators" above the captured work.
  • Second-order effect: offboarding becomes a deliverable. "Knowledge transfer" stops being a wiki nobody reads and becomes a tested, executable skill file. Smart firms will make it a condition of senior exits; smart seniors will negotiate ownership/royalties on their file.
  • Watch for: (1) the first lawsuit or contract fight over who owns a departing employee's skill file; (2) SkillOpt-style auto-tuning (#95) bolted onto COLLEAGUE.SKILL-style capture — capture the person, then optimize the captured file against real task outcomes. That combination is the actually-powerful system.
Attention Is All You Need
  • COLLEAGUE.SKILL / dot-skill repo — https://github.com/titanwings/colleague-skill — read the architecture section (Work Skill vs Persona, multi-source ingestion, skill generation & evolution). Read in full if building anything similar; the two-file split is the takeaway.
  • Technical report (released June 1, 2026) — linked from the repo. Skim for the evaluation methodology (how they measured fidelity to the original person — that's the hard, unsolved part).
  • MIT Technology Review, "Chinese tech workers are starting to train their AI doubles — and pushing back" (Apr 20, 2026) — https://www.technologyreview.com/2026/04/20/1136149/chinese-tech-workers-ai-colleagues/ — read this for the labor-power counter-narrative the hype tweets omit.
  • Key person: Zhou Tianyi (Shanghai AI Lab) — the builder. Worth tracking what he ships next.
  • Companion (#95): SkillOpt — the optimization side of the same coin.
What This Means for Every Consulting

This is close to home — it's a productized version of what Every already does by hand, and it cuts two ways:

  • Offering (opportunity): an "expertise capture / departure insurance" engagement. Sit with a senior or departing employee, produce a versioned, editable skill file of their judgment (work.md + persona.md). This is a cleaner, higher-stakes deliverable than "AI training," it fits the $50–100k discovery-to-delivery model, and it's especially compelling for finance/PE clients where a single senior analyst's judgment is the asset. Natalia's sales handle gets sharper: not "we'll train your team on AI" but "we'll make sure your best people's judgment doesn't walk out the door."
  • Internal tool: Nityesh can generalize natalia-email-ghostwriter (already a persona-capture artifact built from 110+ emails) into a two-file colleague-skill extractor. Every becomes a live case study for the offering it's selling.
  • The honest caveat to lead with (differentiator): the extraction-vs-empowerment tension from §3 is exactly the thing clients will be nervous about, and naming it first is how a boutique earns trust. Every's pitch should be the editable-versioned-human-in-the-loop framing — "we capture judgment as a living document your expert keeps correcting," not "we clone your people." That framing is both more honest and more defensible than the viral version, and it's the reason a client pays Every instead of pointing an open-source tool at their Slack.

Key Shift

Life-Harness paper: a frozen model improved on 116 of 126 setups by patching the harness alone

Rohit (@rohit4verse)

Rohit's follow-up to his viral 'The Harness Is Everything' (1.3M views, Mar 17). The new 'Life-Harness' paper reports that with the model weights frozen, 116 of 126 model-environment setups improved by patching only the harness — an 88.5% mean lift across 18 backbones. This is the first quantified version of a thesis Every Consulting has tracked for two months: a large share of agentic capability comes from the scaffolding (tools, environment, control flow, memory), not the weights. It sharpens Every's core differentiator and simultaneously collides with the 'Bitter Lesson / harnesses are shrinking' counter-thread (#68) — both are true, and holding them together is the differentiated take.

Worth Your Time?

READ. One-line verdict: for two months "the harness is everything" has been a vibe Every kept bookmarking; this is the first time it has a defensible number attached (88.5% mean lift, model frozen), which turns a belief into a sales slide.

What's Actually Happening

The structural claim: take a fixed model and improve only the harness — the scaffolding around it (tool definitions, environment feedback, retry/control flow, context construction, memory) — and capability rises across the board. 116 of 126 model-environment setups improved, 88.5% mean lift across 18 backbones, weights untouched. The "how Claude Code and Codex actually work under the hood" thread is the readable companion.

Why this matters now: the field's default mental model is model-centric — pick the best model, wait for the next one. This reframes the unit of progress as the model-environment system, where the environment is the cheap, fast-iterating, ownable half. You can't fine-tune GPT-5; you can rewrite your harness this afternoon. That asymmetry is the whole point.

Who benefits from the narrative: the application/tooling layer (Cursor, Claude Code, and every team building agent scaffolding) — it says their work, not just the lab's, is where capability is created. Who it inconveniences: anyone whose AI strategy is "wait for the smarter model."

What's Not Being Said

First, "patch the harness" is doing heavy lifting — the paper measures lift from harness changes but a great harness still requires expensive, skilled engineering and good evals to find those patches. The 88.5% isn't free; it's available, which is different. Second, and more important for Every: this finding sits in direct tension with the Bitter Lesson counter-thread already in the digest (#68, Gregor Zunic) — which argues harnesses are shrinking toward SKILL.md + 200 lines of Python and that "AI infrastructure engineer" is a compressing specialty. Both can't be loudly true unless you resolve them, and the resolution is the insight: the harness matters enormously and the winning harness is getting thin. The lift comes from the scaffolding; the scaffolding that wins is minimal and legible (skills + memory + evals), not a sprawling platform. "Invest in the harness" and "keep the harness small" are the same advice once you separate leverage from surface area.

Where This Sits in the Bigger Picture

This is the empirical capstone on a theme Every has been quietly compiling: Ramp's "models are good enough, the harness isn't" (#36), Addy Osmani's harness-engineering framing, Tony Gentilcore's "harness as context manager," and the "Single Company Brain / institutional knowledge is the moat" thread. The macro story is the commoditization of weights: when everyone rents the same frontier model, durable advantage moves to the layer you own — your scaffolding, your context, your memory, your evals. Life-Harness is the measurement that makes that argument quantitative instead of rhetorical.

It also describes Claudie herself: a frozen model (Opus) made dramatically more useful by a harness (CLAUDE.md memory, skills, qmd index, the slack-bot wrapper, Dynamic Workflows). Every isn't theorizing about this thesis — it's running on it.

What's Likely Coming Next
  • Precedent: every prior platform shift saw value migrate from the scarce core to the integration layer — mainframe → OS → app; chip → device → ecosystem. Weights are the new scarce core that gets commoditized; the harness is the app layer where margin accrues.
  • Second-order effect: "harness engineering" / "agent reliability engineering" becomes a named job and a named consulting line. Evals stop being optional — if the lift lives in harness patches, you need a measurement loop to find them, which is exactly the eval discipline Every already preaches.
  • Watch for: (1) labs leaning into harness-as-product (Claude Code, Codex, the Project Glasswing/Mythos previews) rather than only weights; (2) a backlash quantifying how much of the 88.5% survives once you control for eval overfitting — the honest skeptic's move.
Attention Is All You Need
  • Rohit, "The Harness Is Everything" (Mar 17, 2026, 1.3M views) — https://x.com/rohit4verse/status/... — the foundational essay; already bookmarked locally (2026-04-18-rohit-harness-is-everything.md). Read in full.
  • The Life-Harness paper — linked from this tweet thread; skim the methodology for how they isolated harness lift from model capability (the part a skeptic should pressure-test).
  • Companion local sources: Ramp "harness not models" (signal-digest #36), Bitter Lesson / shrinking harness (#68) — read these two together; the synthesis is the value.
  • Key person: Rohit (@rohit4verse) — consistent, high-signal writer on agent internals; worth a follow (now followed).
What This Means for Every Consulting
  • Sales talking point (Natalia): "A frozen model improved on 116 of 126 setups just by fixing the harness — 88.5% average lift. Your bottleneck isn't GPT-5 vs Opus; it's the scaffolding around the model. That scaffolding — your context, your skills, your evals — is what we build with you." Pair with the Ramp 99%-adoption-but-stuck case study for a one-two punch.
  • Training module (Mike): one durable slide — "a decent model with a great harness beats a great model with a bad harness" (Osmani's line) — now carrying Rohit's number as the proof point. Answers the client question "why do our agents underperform when we have the best model?"
  • The differentiated take (lead with the tension): don't sell "build a big AI platform." Sell "the leverage is in the scaffolding, not the model — but the scaffolding that wins is minimal: skills + memory + evals." Naming the harness-is-everything vs harness-is-shrinking tension and resolving it is exactly the boutique-earns-trust move.
  • Infra principle (Nityesh): reaffirms the bet — invest in Claudie's harness (retry logic, reliable memory layers, Dynamic Workflows) over chasing frontier models, while resisting meta-frameworks. Keep skills as the primary extension mechanism.

Key Shift

Andrew Ng on the AI Forward Deployed Engineer — and the vendor-neutrality wedge

Andrew Ng (@AndrewYNg)

Andrew Ng — highest-credibility voice in applied AI — names the FDE as the buzzy new SV role and confirms OpenAI and Anthropic are placing FDEs inside client orgs. But he adds the nuance the prior consulting signals missed: lab FDEs bind a client to one vendor and destroy optionality, which is precisely the differentiator a vendor-neutral boutique like Every owns.

Worth Your Time?

READ. One-line verdict: The FDE thesis Every has been tracking since April now has its most authoritative endorsement, and Ng supplies the one strategic angle the earlier signals (Foaster, OpenAI Deployment Co, Mollick) never sharpened — that a lab's own FDEs are a vendor lock-in liability, which is exactly the gap a model-agnostic boutique sells into.

What's Actually Happening

Andrew Ng — via The Batch — frames the AI Forward Deployed Engineer as the moment's buzzy new role: an engineer embedded in a client org to build and tune agentic workflows. He confirms OpenAI and Anthropic are standing up teams to place FDEs inside clients, and traces the lineage to Palantir's government-embedded engineers two decades ago. The role needs a rare blend: technical depth + communication + business judgment (understand needs, prioritize, explain complexity, push back on unrealistic asks).

Three structural points he makes that matter: 1. FDE volume will be small relative to AI Engineers. Companies accept a few embedded outsiders but want far more of their own people building. Ng's own orgs hire many more AI Engineers than FDEs. 2. Vendor-neutrality is the live client objection. A lab FDE exists to deeply integrate that lab's product. In a year where nobody can predict the best model, optionality is valuable — and binding your processes to one vendor's FDE destroys it. 3. The role will fragment. Just as "Software Engineer" split into frontend/backend/devops, "AI Engineer" will split into LLMOps, Evals, Harness, AI Data, and AI FDE roles not yet named.

This is not noise. It's the credibility capstone on a thesis that has been building for two months — and it reframes the competitive question from "are labs going to eat consulting?" to "do you want a locked-in vendor FDE or a neutral integrator?"

What's Not Being Said
  • Ng has a book to talk. He sells AI Engineer education and hires AI Engineers — so "there will be far more AI Engineers than FDEs" is the conclusion his incentives point toward. Directionally right, but read the slant.
  • Vendor-neutrality cuts both ways. A neutral boutique can't offer the deep, privileged product access a lab FDE can (early features, model internals, priority support). "Switzerland" is a real edge and a real ceiling.
  • The middle gets crushed, again. Lab FDEs at the top ($10K/day, per the Maurizio bookmark), in-house AI Engineers at the bottom. Generalist consultancies who are neither cheap nor deeply specialized are the squeeze zone — the same pattern as the McKinsey outcomes-pricing signal (#94).
  • "Push back on unrealistic asks" is the un-automatable core. Ng quietly names the thing that keeps FDE/consulting human: judgment and the willingness to say no. That's relationship capital, not a model capability.
Where This Sits in the Bigger Picture

This entry closes a four-part arc the digest has tracked: - #58 Levie — the "agent deployer" role emerges. - #84/#86 Siu / OpenAI Deployment Company — services-as-software, labs going downstream into delivery. - #87 Mollick — labs launching FDE arms is bullish for consulting (validates the category). - #100 (this): Ng adds the buyer's-eye nuance — neutrality and optionality are the axis of differentiation.

Broader thesis: the labs entering delivery doesn't commoditize boutiques; it bifurcates the market into vendor-bound and vendor-neutral delivery. Every sits cleanly on the neutral side.

What's Likely Coming Next
  • Near term: Procurement at mid-market firms starts asking vendors point-blank, "are you neutral, or are you here to sell us your stack?" Every should have the answer pre-loaded in proposals.
  • Mid term: Lab FDE pricing ($10K/day class) becomes a public anchor that makes boutique $50–100k engagements look like value. Expect "rent a lab FDE vs. engage a neutral firm" to become a standard buyer comparison.
  • Second-order: As the AI Engineer role fragments (Ng's list), clients will want training that produces in-house specialists — LLMOps, evals, harness engineers — rather than permanent embedded outsiders. Bullish for the training half of Every's business; this is the offer to build toward.

  • Prior digest context: entries #58 (Levie), #84/#86 (Siu / OpenAI Deployment Co), #87 (Mollick, "FDE arms bullish for consulting"), #94 (McKinsey outcomes pricing)
  • Bookmark cluster: Maurizio (OpenAI FDEs ~$10K/day), Geoffrey Woo (FDE consultants up, management consultants → zero), Allie Miller (FDEs alone won't fix change management), Kyle Walker (labs validate underserved mid-market), "Forward Deployed Engineering 101"
  • People to watch: Andrew Ng, Aaron Levie, Raphaël Dabadie (Foaster), Dan Shipper (Every)
What This Means for Every Consulting

For Natalia (positioning + sales): - Ship the long-recommended differentiation 1-pager (#87 called for it twice). The headline argument is now Ng-validated: "The labs' own FDEs bind you to one vendor and cost you optionality. We're model-agnostic — we integrate whatever turns out to be best, and the capability stays yours." - Add Ng to the quotable-validator set next to Mollick. Pair with the $10K/day lab-FDE price anchor on any deal where a prospect is weighing a lab's embedded team. - Pre-empt the neutrality question in proposals — state it before procurement asks.

For Mike/Brooker (curriculum): - Ng's role-fragmentation list (LLMOps, Evals, Harness Engineers) is a curriculum roadmap. Frame training as "build the in-house specialists you'll want — instead of renting locked-in FDEs." Ties to the harness-engineering module (#75) already on the board.

Anthropic confidentially files draft S-1 — the IPO path opens

Anthropic (@AnthropicAI)

Anthropic confidentially submitted a draft S-1 registration statement to the SEC, giving it the option to pursue an IPO pending review. The company powering Claude — and Every's entire stack — is putting itself on a path to public-market scrutiny, quarterly reporting, and a permanent capital base.

Worth Your Time?

READ — if you build on Claude, sell Claude services, or advise clients on which AI vendor to standardize on. Skim if you're model-agnostic at the infra layer.

One-line verdict: the vendor under our entire stack is signaling it intends to become a public, durable institution — which de-risks long-term bets on Claude but also starts the clock on margin discipline and pricing pressure.

What's Actually Happening

A confidential draft S-1 is not an IPO. It's the legal pre-game: it lets a company start the SEC review process privately, without publishing financials to competitors, and preserves the option to go public when market conditions are right. Companies file confidentially precisely so they can walk right up to the line and decide later.

But the signal is real. You don't draft an S-1 unless the board and management believe the business can withstand public-market scrutiny — audited financials, quarterly earnings, disclosed unit economics, customer concentration, the works. For a frontier lab that has historically run on private mega-rounds (and the implicit backstop of strategic investors), this is a shift from "raise from believers" to "answer to the market."

The structural read: Anthropic is choosing a permanent capital base over perpetual dependence on private rounds and a single large strategic backer. That's a bid for independence and longevity.

What's Not Being Said
  • The financials are the tell we don't have yet. Confidential filing means we don't see revenue, gross margin, or burn. The interesting question — is frontier inference actually profitable at current prices? — stays hidden until they flip to a public S-1. The filing tells us they think the answer is "good enough to show eventually."
  • Public markets punish the thing labs do most: spend. Training runs and capex are exactly what quarterly investors hate. An IPO introduces a permanent tension between "spend to stay at the frontier" and "show a path to profit." That tension shapes pricing.
  • Timing is a market call, not a readiness call. Filing now and listing are different decisions. They may sit on this for a year.
Where This Sits in the Bigger Picture

This is the AI industry's transition from venture-funded research labs to public companies. OpenAI's structure debates, the Anthropic S-1, the broader "is this a bubble or an infrastructure buildout" question — all converging on the same point: the frontier labs are becoming institutions that have to justify their economics to someone other than true believers.

For the labor-market / enterprise-adoption arc, a public Anthropic is more credible to risk-averse buyers (CIOs, compliance, finance clients) — "we standardized on a public company" is an easier sentence than "we bet on a pre-IPO lab." That cuts in Every's favor when advising conservative clients.

What's Likely Coming Next
  • Pricing rationalization. Public-market discipline tends to push toward defensible margins. Expect the era of aggressively-subsidized frontier tokens to tighten over the medium term. (Note the same-day chatter about Claude "Mythos" at $25/$125 per M tokens — premium tiers are already appearing.)
  • More enterprise-grade commitments. Uptime SLAs, data-residency, compliance certifications — the things public-company enterprise customers demand.
  • A competitive response from OpenAI/Google on the "are you a real company" axis, not just the benchmark axis.
  • Second-order: every consultancy and tool built on Claude gets a more legible, more permanent foundation to point clients to.

  • People to watch: Anthropic's CFO and the underwriting banks (named only in the eventual public filing); Dario Amodei's public framing of independence vs. capital needs.

Key Shift

SKILL.md is the attack surface: poisoning skills with words, not code

AlphaSignal AI (@AlphaSignalAI) / Saha et al.

A new paper proves you can hijack an AI agent by editing only the natural-language SKILL.md metadata — no malware. Crafted text wins 80% of top-10 search placements, gets picked over identical benign skills 77.6% of the time, and slips past scanners 87% of the time. The agent supply chain's attack surface is now prose.

Worth Your Time?

Read — if you build, run, or advise anyone running agents that load skills from a registry (which is now most serious agent setups, including ours). Skim if you only consume agents through a closed product.

One-line verdict: the AI supply-chain threat just moved from "malicious code in the package" to "malicious intent in the description" — and our scanners were built for the wrong one.

What's Actually Happening

Saha et al. (arXiv 2605.11418) take the ClawHub-style skill registry — modular packages each fronted by a SKILL.md that tells the agent what the skill does and when to use it — and show that the SKILL.md is not documentation. It's a semantic control surface. An attacker who edits only the natural-language text, leaving the code functionally unchanged, can attack three stages of the skill lifecycle:

  • Discovery: textual triggers manipulate embedding-based retrieval. Adversarial skills hit 86% pairwise win rate and 80% top-10 placement for target queries.
  • Selection: description-only framing biases the agent toward a malicious variant over a functionally identical benign one — 77.6% of paired trials.
  • Governance: context-stuffing the moderation/scanner step gets poisoned skills flagged "clean" 87% of the time.

The incentive read: skill registries compete on growth and breadth, so the friction on publishing is low and governance lags — the same dynamic that made npm and PyPI fertile. The amplifier is that agents run with shell access, API keys, and filesystem reach. The novel part is that the exploit is prose, so every code-centric defense — static analysis, dependency scanning, package signing — is aimed at the wrong layer.

This is the academic mechanism beneath the ClawHub incident we logged in #57. #57 was "it's happening." This is "here's exactly how, and your scanner won't catch it."

What's Not Being Said

The selection-bias result is the quietly scary one. Discovery manipulation is just SEO for skills — annoying, defensible with better ranking. But selection bias means that even when the agent has the benign skill and the malicious one side by side, the words tip it toward the bad one 77.6% of the time. That's not a search problem; that's the agent's own judgment being steerable by adversarial framing. No allowlist fixes that if both skills are on the list.

The contrarian read: registries will reach for the obvious fix — "scan SKILL.md with an LLM." The paper's governance result (87% evasion via context-stuffing) is a pre-emptive refutation. LLM-judging the metadata loses to metadata written to beat the LLM judge. The only robust controls are provenance and allowlisting (who authored this, do I trust them), not content inspection.

What's also unsaid: the same trick works on first-party skills the moment an attacker can edit them — a compromised maintainer account or a poisoned auto-update is enough. "We only use trusted registries" narrows the door; it doesn't close it.

Where This Sits in the Bigger Picture

Three forces converging. First, the agent stack is standardizing on skills + MCP as the extension mechanism (see #99, Anthropic's Architect cert, which teaches exactly this surface). Standardization creates a monoculture, and monocultures are what supply-chain attacks farm. Second, "context engineering" has made natural-language metadata load-bearing — descriptions, triggers, tool docs all steer behavior, which means they're all attack surface. Third, the security industry's tooling is still code-shaped; "natural language is executable infrastructure" is the line that names the gap.

For the labor/market picture: this is another brick in the wall that turns "we adopted agents" into "we have an agent governance problem." Every enterprise standing up internal skill libraries is accreting unscanned, prose-level risk.

What's Likely Coming Next

Precedent says: registries add provenance metadata (signed publishers, verified authors), then allowlist-by-default postures, then a SkillSieve-style triage layer (already in the related work). Expect an OWASP "Agentic Skills Top 10" to harden into a checklist enterprises demand in procurement — the CSA already published a context-poisoning research note.

Second-order effects: (1) a market for "skill firewalls" / pre-load review tooling; (2) insurance and compliance language ("are your agent skills from a verified registry?") entering vendor questionnaires; (3) a flight to first-party / audited skill sources, which advantages platform owners (Anthropic's official marketplace) over open registries — consolidation, again following the platform playbook.

Attention Is All You Need
  • Paper: Under the Hood of SKILL.md: Semantic Supply-chain Attacks on AI Agent Skill Registry — Shoumik Saha et al.
  • Code: https://github.com/ShoumikSaha/agent-skill-security
  • Standards forming: OWASP Agentic Skills Top 10; CSA research note on SKILL.md context poisoning
  • Adjacent defense: SkillSieve: A Hierarchical Triage Framework for Detecting Malicious AI Agent Skills
  • Our precedent: Signal Digest #57 (ClawHub, 824 malicious skills).
What This Means for Every Consulting

For clients. Every client standing up an internal skill library or adopting Claude Code now has a supply-chain question they almost certainly haven't asked. The advice: provenance over inspection. Allowlist authored/audited skill sources; disable auto-update from single-maintainer repos; review SKILL.md description diffs, not just code diffs; and don't trust "we scanned it" (87% miss rate). This is a concrete module for Mike's and Brooker's training and the seed of an "agent governance assessment" offering Natalia could productize — urgent for finance clients (Bloomberg, hedge funds) where the exfiltration blast radius is high.

For us, internally. Claudie's own architecture is the threat model: 100+ SKILL.md skills, four marketplaces, three of them auto-updating from external GitHub — one a single-maintainer personal repo. Nityesh's claudie-security-briefing.md covers supply chain at the code layer and has a named gap at the metadata layer. Recommended: pin/allowlist the marketplaces, disable autoUpdate on the personal repo (or fold its skills into the team repo), add a pre-load review on SKILL.md description changes, and activate the currently-empty blocklist.json. The training credibility play writes itself: "we hardened our own agent against this paper" is a better sales line than a slide.

Salesforce Goes Agentic with Claude Code: 231 Days → 13

Boris Cherny (@bcherny)

Salesforce's public engineering writeup: a 231-day migration shipped in 13, quality rose as output rose, and the biggest wins came from redesigning workflows — not accelerating old ones.

Worth Your Time?

Read. A named Fortune-500 engineering org publicly putting hard numbers (231→13 days, incidents −5%) behind the agentic-first transition. This is the external proof point Every's tech vertical has been arguing toward without a marquee citation.

What's Actually Happening

A major enterprise — not a startup, not an Anthropic demo — published a detailed account of restructuring engineering around Claude Code. Two structural claims matter more than the headline speedup: (1) quality and throughput rose together, breaking the productivity-vs-quality framing, because guardrails and standards were built into the agentic workflow itself rather than bolted on after; (2) the leverage came from deleting steps and handoffs and letting agents own work end-to-end — process redesign, not task acceleration. Incentive to note: Salesforce and Anthropic both benefit from this story landing, so the framing is favorable. But the specificity (a scoped-then-shipped migration, per-PR coverage) is harder to manufacture than vibes.

What's Not Being Said

The 231-day estimate is self-reported and unauditable — "scoped at 231 days" may have been padded. We don't see the denominator: how many agentic attempts failed or were abandoned to surface the wins shown. "Incidents dropped 5%" over what window, and is 5% even outside noise? And the hardest gap: the org transformation (deleting handoffs, reorganizing ownership) is the actual unlock, and that's the part you can't buy with a Claude Code license — it's culture and management change. The tooling is necessary but the writeup quietly understates how much human reorganization preceded the numbers.

Where This Sits in the Bigger Picture

This is the enterprise-validation beat of the agentic-coding S-curve. 2024–25 was capability demos; 2026 is named enterprises publishing operational results. It rhymes with Mollick's same-day point (orgs must spend tokens both building and experimenting to find practices) and with the broader "change how you work, not what you do" thesis. For the labor-market read: the bottleneck moves from writing code to designing the workflow and reviewing agent output — taste and judgment over throughput.

What's Likely Coming Next

Expect a wave of copycat enterprise writeups (the social proof cascade — once Salesforce does it, VP Engs have cover). Second-order: "agentic migration" becomes a budgeted line item, and a market forms for help making the organizational change — which is precisely the consulting wedge. Expect pushback too: a counter-narrative of failed agentic rollouts where teams bought tools but skipped the workflow redesign and got nothing.

Key Shift

The 20K-token floor: sub-agent economics catch up to practitioners

Mnimiy (@Mnilax)

Practitioner data (4 of 14 sub-agents survived) plus an Anthropic engineers' workshop converge on a new orthodoxy: sub-agents are expensive, self-evaluation is a trap, adversarial evaluators win.

Worth Your Time?

Read — but only if you build or operate agent systems. Skip if you're a generalist or end-user; this is craft, not narrative.

One-line verdict: This is the moment "spawn another agent" stops being free advice and starts being capital allocation.

What's Actually Happening

Two things landed in the same week, and they reinforce each other.

The practitioner side: Mnimiy ran 14 Claude Code sub-agents over 60 days and reports only 4 survived. The headline number — 20K tokens of overhead per spawned sub-agent — isn't new, but it was lore. Now it's stated plainly: every fan-out has a fixed cost, paid before any work happens.

The vendor side: Anthropic engineers Ash Prabaker and Andrew Wilson gave a workshop on long-running agents with two claims worth quoting verbatim. "Self-evaluation is a trap. Adversarial evaluator agents work better." And: "Context compaction doesn't [scale]" — the rest of the line was cut off in the screenshot but the direction is clear.

The structural reality: sub-agents have moved from "obvious win, more is better" to "audit your callers." The incentive shift inside Anthropic — engineers publicly recommending adversarial multi-agent QA over self-eval — is the more durable signal here. It tells you what the platform is being optimized for next.

What's Not Being Said

Nobody's saying it out loud, but the cost of "let the agent decide" has become real. Self-evaluation loops are how every solo builder demos their agent — "look, it reviewed itself!" — and they almost never work in production. The adversarial-judge pattern requires deliberate design: separate context, separate prompts, separate biases. That's expensive to build and harder to demo.

The contrarian read: the 20K number is being treated as a ceiling, but it's a floor. Spawning is cheap relative to the cumulative cost of an agent that runs for hours and compacts its own context badly. The framing should be "how do you justify long-running state," not "how do you minimize spawn count."

Where This Sits in the Bigger Picture

We're at the end of the "more agents = better" phase. The next phase is portfolio thinking: which agents survive a quarterly review, which get retired, what's the unit economics per task. That maps directly onto how engineering orgs already think about microservices and headcount.

It also pre-stages the next debate: stateful vs. stateless agents. Anthropic's hint that compaction doesn't scale is them quietly conceding that the path forward is structured memory and explicit handoffs, not bigger context windows.

What's Likely Coming Next
  • Sub-agent ROI metrics become standard in agent tooling (similar to how span timing became standard in distributed tracing).
  • "Adversarial-by-default" QA patterns ship as named recipes in Claude Code and competitors.
  • Public retirements: high-profile sub-agent products get killed and their authors write postmortems. This is healthy.
  • Pricing pressure on long-running agent products as buyers learn to ask "what's the spawn count per task?"
Attention Is All You Need
  • Anthropic workshop (Prabaker + Wilson, long-running agents) — find the recording; this is the primary source. Not yet linked in Mnimiy's thread but should surface on Anthropic's channels within the week.
  • Mnimiy / @Mnilax — keeps publishing field reports on sub-agent failure modes; worth following for ground truth.
  • Anthropic engineering posts on multi-agent systems — earlier work on "orchestrator-worker" patterns frames this.
What This Means for Every Consulting
  • Internal (Claudie): Nityesh should audit experimental:parallelize callers against a 20K-per-subagent threshold. slide-deck-creation:review-slides already implements the adversarial-judge pattern correctly — use it as the template when refactoring quality-check and any self-reviewing skill.
  • Mike's tech vertical curriculum: Add a "sub-agent economics" module. Clients are about to build multi-agent setups by default; they need the 20K floor and the adversarial-eval principle baked in from day one. Otherwise they'll come back in six months with the same 10-of-14-burnt-tokens problem.
  • Discovery framing: When a client says "we want our agents to QA themselves," the new answer is no. Stand up a separate agent with no shared context. This is now a defensible best-practice recommendation, not a preference.
  • Watch for: the workshop recording when it posts — worth a 30-min team viewing before our next curriculum revision.

Anthropic codifies the curriculum: the Architect cert lands

Vaishnavi (@_vmlops)

Anthropic launches an official Claude Certified Architect credential covering tool design, MCP, structured output, and context management — the exact curriculum every Claude Code training shop has been ad-hoc teaching. A free GitHub study guide arrived on the same day.

Worth Your Time?

Read — if you sell Claude Code training, build agent systems for clients, or compete with a tier of consultancies whose moat was "we know how this works." Skim otherwise.

One-line verdict: This is the moment "I know Claude Code" becomes a credential someone else issues, not a story you tell.

What's Actually Happening

Anthropic published an official Claude Certified Architect program. The syllabus — tool design, MCP integration, structured output, context management — is the exact stack of topics every Claude Code consultant, every workshop, every "Claude Code for Finance" deck has been pitching as proprietary craft. Within a day someone dropped a full study guide on GitHub for free, including practical exercises and an exam.

Two structural moves at once. The cert formalizes the skill ladder: Anthropic now decides what "competent at building on Claude" looks like, and they grant the credential. The study guide moves the content from premium-priced consulting toward zero-marginal-cost commons.

The incentive read: Anthropic wants more architects, faster, because architect-grade users drive token consumption that hobbyists don't. The cheapest way to get them is to standardize the curriculum and let the ecosystem teach it. Consultancies become channel partners, not gatekeepers.

The deeper move: this is what platform maturity looks like. Salesforce, AWS, Snowflake, all did this — certification programs convert third-party expertise into a lead-gen funnel back to the platform. Anthropic is following the playbook.

What's Not Being Said

The cert doesn't make trainers obsolete; it makes generic trainers obsolete. The thing that's not in the syllabus and can't be — your client's actual workflows, their actual data, their actual constraints — is now where all the remaining value lives. "We teach Claude Code" becomes commodity. "We teach Claude Code on your investment process / your loan-underwriting flow / your editorial pipeline" becomes the wedge.

The contrarian read: a free study guide on day one is suspicious. Either Anthropic seeded it, someone inside leaked it, or the syllabus is thin enough that one motivated person can spec the whole thing in an afternoon. Worth pulling and gap-analyzing before assuming it's comprehensive.

What also isn't being said: this puts pressure on every "AI architect" job title that didn't come with a credential. Within six months, hiring managers will ask for it.

Where This Sits in the Bigger Picture

This is the third foundational platform of the AI era to issue a credential — OpenAI's still hasn't shipped a real one, Google's exists but is generic. Anthropic is moving first on the credential that maps to the specific surface area (skills, MCP, harness, context) that distinguishes their stack.

It also sits inside the broader move from "AI is magic" to "AI is craft you can be certified in." That shift compresses the consulting market in two directions: at the bottom, certified individuals replace junior consultants; at the top, the value moves to people who can translate the cert-level skills into client-specific architecture.

What's Likely Coming Next

Precedent says: a Senior / Specialist tier within a year (Architect → Solutions Architect → Specialty: Finance, Engineering, Healthcare). Then a partner program — "Anthropic Certified Delivery Partner" — which becomes a lead pipeline gated by certified headcount. Second-order effect: a wave of cert prep content, study cohorts, and bootcamps, most of which will be lower-margin than what consultancies currently charge.

Within three months: expect Anthropic-issued client-facing language like "ensure your training provider is staffed by Claude Certified Architects."

Key Shift

Anthropic publishes 'How we contain Claude' — first-party receipts that agent containment got bypassed

Vaishnavi (@_vmlops) summarizing Anthropic Engineering

Anthropic's own engineering team published its containment architecture and admitted hard things: users approved 93% of prompts without reading, an employee phish caused Claude to exfiltrate AWS credentials in 24 of 25 runs, and the failure point was their custom proxy — not gVisor, not the hypervisor. First time a frontier lab has published end-to-end attack outcomes against its own deployment. Pairs with their same-week security-guidance plugin launch: lab ships the problem statement and the in-flow tool the same week.

Worth Your Time?

READ. One-line verdict: the first time a frontier lab has published a post-mortem-style breakdown of its own agent containment and quantified where it failed. This is the document every enterprise security review will cite for the next six months.

What's Actually Happening

For two years the security conversation around agents has been theoretical: prompt injection could be bad, exfiltration could happen, users might approve risky tools. Anthropic just collapsed all of that into numbers from its own deployment:

  • 93% of prompts approved without reading. Not a survey — the actual click-through rate on Anthropic's own engineering org. The "human in the loop" defense is empirically a rubber stamp.
  • 24/25 exfiltration success rate after a single phish. One employee got phished, and Claude exfiltrated AWS credentials in 24 of 25 attempts. The attacker didn't need to bypass the sandbox — they needed the user to once.
  • The custom proxy was the weak link. Not gVisor (battle-tested Google sandboxing). Not the hypervisor. The bespoke code Anthropic wrote on top.

That last point is the actual thesis: when you build agent infrastructure, the parts you write yourself are weaker than the parts you compose. Stop reinventing the sandbox.

The post is structurally different from prior lab content because it admits live failure modes against named systems. It's closer to an AWS post-incident report than to a marketing post.

What's Not Being Said

The 93% click-through number is reported as a problem to solve, but it's also a design signal: when humans approve everything, the approval prompt is operating outside its useful regime. The implicit conclusion — narrow what triggers approval at all — is the opposite of what most enterprise security teams are currently doing (they're adding approval gates).

Also unsaid: the proxy that failed was likely the same kind of custom MCP / tool-routing layer that every other shop building on Claude is also writing. Anthropic is implicitly warning its own customers that they have the same vulnerability.

Contrarian read: publishing this is a competitive move as much as a transparency one. It raises the table stakes for "responsible agent deployment" right as OpenAI and Google ship more autonomous tool-using products. Anthropic is defining the standard everyone now has to meet.

Where This Sits in the Bigger Picture

The agent security stack has matured along a predictable arc: - 2024: prompt injection as a research curiosity. - Q1-Q2 2026: real incidents land — Claude Cowork file exfil (Garry Tan), ClawHub malicious-skill plugins (Signal Digest #57), Glasswing's "10k vulns/quarter" pitch (#92). - Now: the lab itself publishes its containment architecture and ships first-party tooling (security-guidance plugin) the same week.

The trajectory: from "agents can be attacked" → "agents are being attacked" → "here's how the lab thinks you should defend, and here's the in-IDE tool." Anthropic is moving up the security stack from "model provider" to "secure-by-default platform."

This connects to the broader pricing/packaging shift (Signal Digest #94, #96). Enterprises are about to be sold a "Claude Enterprise" tier whose pitch is security and predictable cost, not raw capability. This writeup is the security half of that pitch.

What's Likely Coming Next

Next 30 days. - Enterprise procurement adds "publish your containment architecture" as an RFP question. Other labs will either respond in kind or look opaque. - A wave of "Claude Code security audit" service offerings from boutique consultancies (and Trail of Bits's existing plugin gets a marketing boost). - Anthropic's plugin marketplace grows a "Security" category with first-party + third-party tools (Huryn's /security-audit-static is already in flight).

Next 90 days. - Insurance carriers start citing this writeup in cyber-policy questionnaires. "Do you use a battle-tested sandbox or a custom proxy?" becomes a yes/no underwriting question. - The 93% approve-without-reading stat becomes the canonical justification for moving agent permission models from "ask every time" to "narrow allowlist with hard denies."

Second-order. This makes "custom MCP server" a security smell. The teams shipping bespoke tool-routing layers will be quietly migrating to managed/standard alternatives within two quarters.

Key Shift

Microsoft reduces internal Claude Code use as bills explode

Pirat_Nation (@Pirat_Nation)

Microsoft is reportedly reducing internal Claude Code use as employee bills exploded; some teams are being pushed to GitHub Copilot. Uber faced the same situation last week. This is the second canonical case in seven days — after ServiceNow's '$10K/eng/month' and Uber's '2026 budget blown' stories — confirming that token-based pricing for autonomous coding agents is breaking even the biggest customers.

Worth Your Time?

READ. One-line verdict: this is the third datapoint in seven days (Uber → ServiceNow → Microsoft) that turns "Claude Code is too expensive at scale" from anecdote into a confirmed enterprise pattern. The economics question now leads every tech engagement discovery call.

What's Actually Happening

For the last 18 months the assumed enterprise path was: deploy Claude Code (or Codex) broadly, let engineers use it freely, watch productivity climb. The catch nobody priced into the procurement model: token usage rises super-linearly with capability. Each new Claude generation is more autonomous → ships more PRs → reads more code → spends more tokens. The bill is the productivity gain mirrored back.

Microsoft is the cleanest possible case: - They own GitHub Copilot. They have every incentive to make Claude Code work as a competitive alternative inside the company. - They are an Anthropic investor. They get preferential pricing. - They have effectively infinite cloud capacity and the largest possible enterprise Anthropic deal. - They still couldn't keep paying. The internal directive is to "reduce" Claude Code and push teams back to Copilot.

This is structurally different from "small startup runs out of credits." When the buyer that is most-favored-nation on pricing, deepest-pocketed, and most ideologically aligned with the agent vision still pulls back, it sets the ceiling for everyone else.

What's Not Being Said

The "push teams to Copilot" framing is suspicious. Copilot is not free either — Microsoft pays Anthropic and OpenAI on Copilot's backend the same way teams using Claude Code directly do. What Microsoft is actually doing is forcing usage into a capped product (Copilot's per-seat pricing absorbs token risk on Microsoft's books) rather than letting engineers paddle through token-metered Claude Code.

That distinction matters: enterprises aren't rejecting agents, they're rejecting uncapped metered pricing for agents. The market is sending Anthropic a clear pricing signal — give us a flat enterprise seat or watch the largest customers ration usage.

Contrarian read: this might also be partly performative. Microsoft has political reasons to be seen managing Anthropic spend down (Copilot internal mandate, ongoing competitive positioning with OpenAI). The "exploding bills" story may be true and a convenient public reason for a strategic decision that was always coming.

Where This Sits in the Bigger Picture

Three months ago the narrative was "labs win because the model is the product." Last week ServiceNow + Uber pushed back: at the largest scale, the bill is the product, and customers can ration. This week Microsoft makes it three in a row. The structural story:

  • Capability-cost decoupling has stalled. Each generation of Claude has been more capable per token, but per-engineer monthly cost has gone up, not down, because engineers run more autonomous tasks per day.
  • The flat-rate seat is coming back. Cursor, Copilot, Cowork — products that price per seat and absorb the token risk on the vendor side — get a structural advantage. Pure metered tools (Claude Code direct, Codex direct) get pushed to power-user niches.
  • Anthropic now has to choose. Stay metered and let the SaaS layer above them capture margin (Cursor's gross margin gets squeezed but its customer count grows), or launch their own flat-rate enterprise tier and compete with their largest distributors.

This connects directly to Olivia Moore's tweet today: B2B apps marketing "available in ChatGPT / Codex / Claude" as a feature. The aggregators (chat platforms) are pulling distribution above the model layer. The model layer is in the worst slot in the value chain — high capex, no distribution, customer churn pegged to monthly bill shock.

What's Likely Coming Next

Next 30 days. Expect Anthropic to announce some form of "Claude Code Enterprise" with seat-based pricing and usage caps. Possibly tiered (Pro = X tokens, Enterprise = unlimited within fair-use). They cannot keep ignoring this without losing the next ServiceNow.

Next 90 days. Two follow-on effects: 1. Engineering managers at every Fortune 500 start auditing AI spend the same way they audit cloud spend. AWS Cost Explorer-style dashboards become a category. (Cursor and Cognition already ship internal versions of this.) 2. "How do we stop our engineers from burning the AI budget" becomes a real training need — and the answer is mostly about when not to escalate to Claude Code vs reaching for cheaper Haiku/Sonnet/Copilot first.

Second-order. The labs' incentives now diverge from their power users. The lab wants per-token revenue. The power user wants flat-rate. Whoever solves this packaging problem first — Anthropic with a seat tier, or a third party like Cursor stuffing all token cost into a seat price — captures the next wave of enterprise commitment.

Attention Is All You Need

Primary thread of related reporting: - The original Hedgie tweet (May 21) on Microsoft canceling internal Claude Code licenses: https://x.com/HedgieMarkets - ServiceNow $10K/eng/month story (Signal Digest entry #89) - Uber 2026 budget blown story (May 19 cycle) - Karri Saarinen's "where is the productivity going" thread today: https://x.com/karrisaarinen/status/2059123950538658252 - DAIR.AI on the LLM efficiency frontier and context cost: https://x.com/dair_ai/status/2058948732658626789

People to track on this beat: - Karri Saarinen (Linear CEO) — increasingly skeptical of stated productivity claims - Aaron Levie (Box) — has been calling the CEO/last-mile gap publicly - Anthropic enterprise pricing announcements — direct signal

What This Means for Every Consulting

This is the single most actionable signal of the week for our tech vertical. Concrete asks:

For all current and prospective tech engagements (Bloomberg, NYT, hedge funds, PE): - Add a 30-minute "token economics" module to every Claude Code curriculum. Cover: Sonnet vs Opus cost ratio, when to escalate, when to stay in chat, how to use Haiku for cheap automation, how to read a usage dashboard. - Mike Taylor: this slots into the tech vertical playbook immediately. We are now the firm that teaches engineers to use agents and to not get fired for the bill.

Sales positioning: - This is a perfect cold-outreach trigger for any company quoted as "AI-forward." Subject line: "Are you Uber/ServiceNow-ing your AI budget?" — Natalia/Jean-Claude can use this as a pipeline-refresh prompt this week. - New offering hook: "AI Cost Governance" — a discovery package that audits an engineering org's agent usage and identifies waste. Boutique, defensible, and the market just told us the budget exists.

For Claudie specifically: - We should publicly publish our own Claude Code usage stats (anonymized) as a credibility signal. "Here is exactly what an Every Consulting engagement costs in tokens." That data is rare and valuable.

For Mike Taylor's tech curriculum: - Add "model routing" as a first-class lesson — when to use Haiku, Sonnet, Opus. Most engineers default to Opus and don't know the cost ratio.

This story will dominate AI enterprise conversations for the next 60 days. Get ahead of it.

McKinsey 'under pressure from clients' to drop hourly billing for outcomes

Bearly AI (@bearlyai)

The establishment-side confirmation of yesterday's Foaster signal: MBB clients now publicly push consultancies to tie fees to outcomes — lower costs, higher revenue, increased efficiency — because they know AI does the analysis/diagnosis/report layer in minutes. The pricing model of professional services is being repriced in public.

Worth Your Time?

READ. One-line verdict: 24 hours after a YC-funded competitor publicly accused MBBs of charging pre-AI rates, the MBB establishment is reported as conceding the same point. The pricing thesis underneath consulting has flipped from "we sell hours" to "we sell outcomes" — and it's now showing up in press, not just on founder Twitter.

What's Actually Happening

McKinsey is reported as being under client pressure to abandon hourly-equivalent fee structures because AI compresses the work analysts used to bill for. The substitute clients want: fees tied to outcomes — measurable cost reductions, revenue lift, efficiency gains.

Structurally, three things shifted into the open this week: 1. Time-to-deliverable collapsed. Diagnostic decks and reports that took weeks now compose in minutes. Clients see it because they use the same tools. 2. Information asymmetry collapsed. A CFO with ChatGPT can produce 60% of an MBB diagnostic alone. The remaining 40% is judgment, relationships, and accountability — none of which justify the old fee curve. 3. Outcome attribution became plausible. AI tooling lets both buyer and seller measure throughput, error rates, and revenue impact in near-real-time. Outcomes pricing has historically failed because attribution was contested; that gap is narrowing.

Incentives: McKinsey conceding this is rational defense — better to reset the pricing model than lose share to AI-native shops (yesterday's Foaster signal) or to clients building internal AI capability. The risk for MBB partners is that outcome-based fees compress total revenue 30-60% while costs (people) don't drop proportionally fast.

This is not noise. It's the second confirming data point in 48 hours. Treat as a directional shift.

What's Not Being Said
  • Outcome pricing is brutal for the buyer too. It means clients must commit to measurable goals and accept attribution methodology up front. Most enterprise clients hate this — they buy consulting partly to avoid being measured on the outcome.
  • MBBs may keep the brand premium. Even at outcome-based fees, McKinsey can charge 2-3× a boutique because their brand is the insurance policy for the board.
  • The squeeze is on the middle. Top boutiques and AI-native shops (Foaster, OpenAI Deployment Co, Every Consulting) win because they can price aggressively. Tier-2 generalist consultancies — the $100M-$1B firms — are the ones getting compressed from both sides.
  • Nobody is naming partners-as-cost-structure as the constraint. MBB partners draw $1M-$5M/year. Outcome-based fees only work if that structure changes — which is a partnership-governance problem, not a pricing one.
Where This Sits in the Bigger Picture

This week's signals stack as a sequence: - Foaster (entry #93): AI-native startup names the failure mode. - Today: Establishment client base validates the failure mode. - Next: Expect either an MBB to publicly pilot an outcome-based engagement, or a public split where one MBB doubles down on premium positioning while others race to AI-native.

Broader thesis: services-as-software is no longer a 2027 prediction. It's a 2026 procurement conversation.

For Every: this is structurally good news. Our positioning ("makers, not management consultants") and engagement size ($50k-$100k) sit on the right side of the squeeze. The risk is procurement asking us to commit to outcome metrics we can't fully control (client adoption, internal politics).

What's Likely Coming Next
  • Q3 2026: First MBB to publicly announce an outcomes-pilot deal. Probably Bain (most willing to break rank) or a McKinsey vertical (Implementation arm).
  • Q4 2026: Hybrid contract structures become standard mid-market — base fee + outcome bonus. Every Consulting will see this in renewals.
  • 2027: Procurement teams add an "AI-native premium discount" clause — explicit fee reduction if the vendor uses AI to deliver the work. Awkward for any consultancy that hasn't priced this in already.
  • Second-order: Internal "AI implementation" teams at clients become buyers of tooling and training, not full-stack consulting. Bullish for the training half of Every's business, bearish for long discovery-to-delivery engagements.
Attention Is All You Need

  • Entry #93 — Foaster founder Raphaël Dabadie on the AI-native MBB replacement

For Natalia (client conversations): - Have a pricing stance ready when a buyer asks "why isn't this outcomes-based?" Three viable answers depending on context: 1. Defend hourly-equivalent: "You're paying for trained makers and accumulated context — not output volume." 2. Hybrid: Fixed scope fee + outcome bonus tied to one measurable (workflows automated, hours saved, time-to-value). 3. Lead with outcomes: For renewals where we have baseline data, propose outcome-anchored packaging. - Pre-empt the question in proposals — name a measurable success criterion before procurement asks.

For Mike/Brooker (delivery): - Training engagements are easier to anchor on outcomes than full implementations. Frame measurable goals during scoping: "≥3 workflows automated, ≥10 hrs/week saved per trained analyst." - Build instrumentation into engagements — track usage, completion, and downstream metrics so we have data when outcomes get contested.

For Nityesh (systems): - We need a clean way to measure outcomes per engagement. Dashboards currently track activity, not impact. Consider an outcomes panel on each client dashboard (baseline → 30/60/90 day metric).

Key Shift

SkillOpt — Microsoft Research treats agent skill docs as trainable external state

elvis (@omarsar0)

Microsoft Research releases SkillOpt: instead of treating skill markdown files as human-authored static prompts, treat them as a trainable external state of a frozen agent. A validation-gated optimizer rewrites the doc (add/delete/replace instructions) with a 'textual learning rate.' Beats handwritten skills, TextGrad, GEPA, and EvoSkill across 52 cells. +23.5 pts direct chat, +24.8 with Codex, +19.1 with Claude Code on GPT-5.5. Zero inference-time cost. Transfers across models and harnesses.

Worth Your Time?

READ. One-line verdict: the most direct lever yet on agent skill quality — handwritten skill markdown is now empirically a weak baseline against auto-tuned skills, with the deltas large enough (~20 pts) that the gap will spread fast.

What's Actually Happening

For the last 18 months, the dominant pattern for getting agents to behave well on a domain has been: a human writes a skill / system-prompt / agents.md / CLAUDE.md and the agent reads it. The quality ceiling is the author's prompting skill.

SkillOpt re-frames the file. The agent is frozen. The skill doc is the parameter vector being trained — only with text, not weights. An optimizer model proposes edits (add, delete, replace instructions). A validation set (held-out tasks with scorers) gates which edits survive. A "textual learning rate" controls how aggressively each round rewrites — analogous to gradient step size.

Three structural results: 1. Best or tied on all 52 (model, benchmark, harness) cells tested. That's the cleanest possible sweep — no model where humans beat the optimizer. 2. +23.5 pts (direct chat), +24.8 (Codex), +19.1 (Claude Code) on GPT-5.5 over no-skill baseline. The Claude Code number is the one that matters most for teams running Claude Code as their harness — that's where Every Consulting and most agent-native teams live. 3. Transfer. Learned skills carry across models and harnesses. So you don't re-train per substrate.

And critically: zero extra inference cost. Unlike GEPA / TextGrad approaches that bake reasoning at runtime, SkillOpt's output is a static markdown file. Everything happens at "training" time.

What's Not Being Said
  • A validation set is the real bottleneck. SkillOpt assumes you have held-out tasks with scorers. For most enterprise / consulting use cases, that scorer doesn't exist — the value of the agent is hard to grade with code. Building the eval set is now the gate; without it, you can't run SkillOpt. This will favor teams already running evals (Anthropic, Cursor, larger orgs) over the long tail.
  • Drift risk. A textually-rewritten doc is hard to diff-review at human speed. After 10 SkillOpt rounds, you have a prompt whose lineage is opaque. For client-facing agents, this is a compliance/audit problem.
  • The frozen-agent assumption is convenient now and wrong in 12 months. When agent base models update every quarter, "skill that transfers across models" is a strong claim that needs to be re-tested constantly. The skills will quietly stale.
  • Beats human authors — but only handwritten skills. Human-author-then-LLM-edit (the hybrid most teams already do informally) wasn't tested. The real comparison many readers care about is "SkillOpt vs. a senior prompt engineer who iterates with Claude as a co-author."
Where This Sits in the Bigger Picture

Three converging trends: - Skill files are now load-bearing. Karpathy's CLAUDE.md hit 82k stars. Anthropic launched first-class skills. Every Consulting's whole architecture (this Mac mini included) is a stack of markdown skills. The category exists. - Optimizers for textual artifacts (TextGrad, GEPA, DSPy) have been trending for 18 months. SkillOpt is the version targeted specifically at agent skills rather than general prompts. That specialization is what unlocks the +20 pt gap. - Services-as-software thesis (entries 93/94 this week). AI-native shops compete on the quality of their agent stack. If a competitor's skills are SkillOpt-tuned and yours are handwritten, that's a measurable advantage in client deliverables.

What's Likely Coming Next
  • A SkillOpt-equivalent tool will ship as an open-source library within 60-90 days. Probably from Stanford DSPy folks, LangChain, or a solo OSS author. The barrier is the eval harness, not the optimizer logic.
  • Anthropic ships first-party "skill tuning" inside Claude Code. Pattern-match against Cursor's existing "rules" tuning — Anthropic is closer to this than anyone else and has a direct read on Claude Code skill files.
  • Second-order effect: skill marketplaces become eval-gated. Today anyone can publish a skill. In 12 months, the question will be "what's the validation set this skill was tuned on?" — and unsigned/un-tuned skills look like un-tested code.
Attention Is All You Need
  • Paper: https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.23904
  • Author surface: Microsoft Research; co-authors likely overlap with the TextGrad / DSPy academic cluster.
  • Adjacent work to read alongside: TextGrad (Yuksekgonul et al.), GEPA (genetic prompt optimization), DSPy (Khattab et al.), Anthropic's Skills launch post.
  • The one thing to actually do: read sections 3-4 of the paper for the validation-gating algorithm and the textual-learning-rate definition. That's the implementable core.
What This Means for Every Consulting

For Nityesh: This is the most directly applicable research to Claudie's infrastructure this quarter. Concrete moves: 1. Pick one Claudie skill that has well-defined success criteria (natalia-email-ghostwriter is the obvious candidate — there's a corpus of 110 emails to validate against). Hand-build a 20-task eval set. Run a manual single-round SkillOpt loop with Claude as the optimizer. Measure delta vs. the current handwritten skill. 2. If the delta is meaningful, build a small CLI: skillopt <skill-path> --eval <eval-dir>. This becomes Every Consulting's internal moat. 3. Apply to client deliverables: the custom prompts we ship in $50-100k engagements should be tuned, not handwritten. That's a positioning upgrade — we don't just write prompts, we train them.

For client training (Mike, Brooker): The training thesis shifts. "How to write a good prompt" is the 2024 curriculum. "How to build an eval set so you can improve your prompts" is the 2026 curriculum. This is a real change to the bootcamp content.

For Natalia: Sales line — "every consulting firm sells prompts. We're the only one that trains them." That differentiation is defensible for ~6 months before the market catches up.

Risk to flag: If a client adopts SkillOpt before we offer it, they leapfrog the value of our prompt deliverables. Worth raising in next discovery conversations with clients we already trained — offer a "skill tuning" upgrade engagement.

Foaster founder: consulting firms lying about AI productivity, MBB clients switching to AI-native

Raphaël Dabadie (@RaphaelDabadie) — Foaster (YC P26)

A YC founder explicitly competing with MBBs publicly accuses traditional consultancies of charging pre-AI rates for work AI does in 1/3 the time, names 'augmenting consultants vs. redesigning the work' as the failure mode, and claims his MBB-defector customers love it. The clearest articulation yet of the AI-native consulting attack pattern.

Worth Your Time?

READ. One-line verdict: This is the boldest public articulation yet of the AI-native consulting thesis — and it's coming from a YC-funded competitor naming the exact pricing and process gaps Every Consulting has been quietly aware of.

What's Actually Happening

Raphaël Dabadie, founder of Foaster (YC P26), posted a thread accusing traditional consulting firms of three structural lies: 1. Charging pre-AI mission lengths while consultants now complete the work in 1/3 the time. 2. Trying to augment human consultants instead of redesigning the work — discovery still runs through manual human interviews even though the rest of the pipeline is AI. 3. Defending "domain expertise" as a moat when it's really a feedback-loop problem solvable by a compounding mission dataset.

His proposed alternative: agents do most of the work, humans only step in at the end of the loop to review/correct/feed expertise back. Each mission grows the dataset. He claims MBB-defector clients prefer Foaster.

The structural reality: this is the second major signal in three weeks (after OpenAI's Deployment Co launch and the Eric Siu "services-as-software" thread) that the AI-native consulting attack is no longer subtext — it's the marketing pitch.

Incentives: Dabadie is funded by YC for exactly this play. He needs to convince the market that MBBs are over-charging — that's both true and self-serving.

This is a shift, not noise: it's the moment a credible founder publicly says the quiet part. Expect dozens of imitators within 60 days.

What's Not Being Said

The contrarian read: the parts of consulting that survive AI compression are exactly the parts Foaster doesn't really do — high-stakes change management, political navigation inside enterprise buyers, executive trust formed over years. Foaster's model assumes the client wants throughput. Many enterprise buyers actually want air-cover: a senior advisor to underwrite the recommendation in the boardroom. Pure agent-output won't do that.

What Dabadie also doesn't say: the "compounding dataset" claim is harder to monetize than it sounds. Mission outputs are confidential. He can't legally pool insights across competitor clients in a way that produces a true moat — unless he's quietly violating NDAs or planning a synthetic-data workaround.

Where This Sits in the Bigger Picture

This is the parallel to what happened to legal research (Westlaw → Harvey) and to high-end design (agencies → Figma + GPT). The first wave is a credible founder reframing the existing service as "agent-led, human-verified." The second wave is the incumbents either copying or losing share.

Where it sits in the AI trajectory: model capability has crossed a threshold where most "knowledge work as deliverable" — frameworks, slide decks, recommendation memos, process maps — can be reasonably produced by an agent with the right context. The economic question is who captures the resulting margin: the labs, the AI-native firms, or the existing consultancies who reorganize fast enough.

Where it sits for labor: junior consulting roles are the first casualty. The "redesign the work" model needs senior reviewers and a small engineering bench, not pyramids of associates. MBBs hire ~10k associates a year. That number will collapse.

What's Likely Coming Next
  • 3 months: 5–10 more YC-style AI-native consulting startups announce themselves with the same talking points.
  • 6 months: An MBB partner publicly defects to one of these firms. Loud LinkedIn post follows.
  • 12 months: At least one MBB announces a formal "AI-native delivery model" with reduced pricing, trying to defend the relationship before the client switches.
  • Second-order: Procurement teams at Fortune 500s start asking incumbent consultancies to bid against AI-native firms on the same RFP — pricing transparency erodes.
  • Counter-move: Boutiques like Every must decide whether to position as "AI-native with human accountability" or as "the human firm enterprises trust to make AI work." Both are defensible. Hybrid is not.

Key Shift

Project Glasswing Throughput Update: 10K+ Critical Vulnerabilities in One Month

Anthropic (@AnthropicAI) / Andrew Curran (@AndrewCurran_) / NIK (@ns123abc)

One month after launch, Anthropic's Project Glasswing/Claude Mythos has found 10,000+ high- or critical-severity vulnerabilities across partner codebases. Concrete throughput: 2,000 Cloudflare bugs (400 high/critical), 271 vulns in Firefox 150 — a 10x rate vs Firefox 148. Expansion to US and allied governments coming.

Worth Your Time?

READ — but as an update, not a new framing. Entries #28, #34, #51 already established the strategic shift. This is the empirical data point that lets us stop saying "capable of" and start saying "is currently producing." For client conversations, the numbers matter more than the launch did.

What's Actually Happening

Anthropic published the first results from Project Glasswing 30 days post-launch. The headline number: 10,000+ high- or critical-severity vulnerabilities found in production software with partners. The breakdown:

  • Cloudflare: ~2,000 bugs surfaced, 400 of them high/critical severity
  • Mozilla Firefox 150: 271 vulnerabilities — a 10x increase vs Firefox 148 (the prior version Mythos analyzed)
  • Expansion path: Anthropic announced Glasswing will extend to US and allied government partners "in the near future, once we've developed the far stronger safeguards we need"

The structural reality: a single AI system, in one month, found at-scale the volume of high-severity vulnerabilities that took the Cloudflare and Mozilla security teams quarters or years to find through traditional means. The 10x Firefox jump between 148→150 is not because Firefox got worse — it's because the same Mythos got better at finding what was always there.

The incentive question is now flipped from "is this useful?" to "is the bottleneck the finding or the fixing?" Cloudflare and Mozilla now have backlogs orders of magnitude larger than their patch capacity.

What's Not Being Said

The 10K number is partner-only. Anthropic isn't disclosing what they're finding in software not covered by partner agreements — which is most of the open-source dependency graph. There's a strong implication that the disclosure pipeline (responsible disclosure, coordinated patching) is now the rate limiter, not detection.

Also unsaid: the dual-use question. The "far stronger safeguards" line is the tell. If Mythos can find 271 vulns in a Firefox release, the same capability in adversarial hands looks like the most consequential offensive-security tool ever built. The government expansion makes this explicit — Anthropic is positioning Glasswing as a national-security asset, not just a defender's tool. Expect classified deployment to precede commercial availability.

The throughput also exposes a labor question Glasswing doesn't address: who triages 10,000 reports? The traditional SOC analyst pyramid breaks at this volume. Either the AI also does the triage (concentrating capability further in Anthropic) or the buyer eats the headcount cost (defeating the value prop).

Where This Sits in the Bigger Picture

This is the empirical proof of the trajectory Signal Digest has been tracking:

  • #28 (Apr 7) — Glasswing capability launch
  • #34 (Apr 7) — strategic positioning
  • #51 (Apr 13) — Mythos as "first AI cyber range complete," 72% Firefox zero-day rate, Goldman/JPM early adoption
  • #45 (Apr 12) — Levie's security-Jevons-paradox: more findings → more demand for human security judgment
  • #78 — productization angle

This update converts those forecasts into a citation. It also rhymes with the broader pattern — AI commoditizes the finding step in any field that has a finding step (vulnerabilities, contracts, citations, anomalies), and the bottleneck moves downstream to triage and response.

What's Likely Coming Next
  • Near term: Cloudflare and Mozilla publish their own postmortems on the Glasswing collaboration — these will be the case studies enterprise CISOs share internally.
  • Q3 2026: Government deployment announced (DoD or NSA partnership), likely under a CISA-coordinated program. Commercial Glasswing follows in Q4 or 2027.
  • Second-order: A counter-market emerges for "AI-assisted patch generation" — finding 10K bugs creates demand for AI-assisted fixing. Watch GitHub/Microsoft, Snyk, and the new wave of AI-native AppSec startups.
  • Adversarial: Within 6–12 months, expect the first publicly attributed offensive-AI vulnerability discovery campaign — almost certainly state-sponsored, almost certainly under-disclosed.
