Key Shift

After Automation: Dan Shipper on why automating everything created more human work

Dan Shipper (@danshipper)

Every automated everything it could with AI agents and grew from 4 to 30 humans. Shipper's structural argument: AI commoditizes yesterday's competence, which floods the market with sameness, which raises the premium on human judgment and framing.

Worth Your Time?

READ. One-line verdict: This is the most credible "AI won't replace your people" argument available to Every Consulting — because it's our own parent company's CEO, with his own headcount data, not a borrowed quote.

What's Actually Happening

Every (the parent company) used Claude and Codex to automate everything automatable — and headcount went from ~4 to 30 since GPT-3. Shipper builds a four-step structural cycle to explain it:

  1. AI commoditizes expertise. Models train on the visible residue of human competence (code, prose, design) and make rare skills cheap and broadly available.
  2. Cheap competence gets adopted everywhere. Operations people open pull requests; marketers make thumbnails; engineers draft articles.
  3. Abundance creates sameness — "slop." Slop is "visible sameness, repeated ad nauseam" — the default when everyone uses identical tools trained on identical data.
  4. Sameness triggers demand for difference. The market quickly recognizes commodity output and starts paying a premium for work that feels "alive and specific, not cheap and generic" — which requires human judgment and situated taste.

The incentive structure underneath: the cycle doesn't terminate. Each saturated capability ("benchmark") gets re-zeroed by a human reframing it one level up. Shipper's sharpest concept is "the frame is not the framer" — even an AGI optimizing flawlessly within a goal still operates inside a frame a human chose. The data point that anchors it: GPT-5.5 scores 62/100 on his internal Senior Engineer benchmark vs. ~85 for humans — and the score collapses further when you strip the "smuggled intelligence" (human judgment) out of the prompt.

This is a genuine shift in framing, not noise — it reframes the entire automation-anxiety conversation from "how many jobs survive" to "which layer of work moves up."

What's Not Being Said

The honest contrarian read: Every is a 30-person AI-native media company with unusually high agency per employee and a founder who writes essays about this for a living. It is not a 5,000-person insurance carrier. The four-step cycle is real, but the transition is not frictionless — the people whose commoditized competence got cheap are not always the same people who get hired into the higher-judgment roles. Shipper's piece is a story about net job creation; it is quiet about distributional pain and retraining lag. The Cloudflare CEO's "middle managers and accountants go first" comment (also circulating this week) is the missing other half.

Also unsaid: the maintenance tax. Shipper mentions one PowerPoint automation with 24 skills, 18 scripts, $62/deck. "Automated" work doesn't vanish — it converts into a different, ongoing engineering load. That's a feature for a consultancy that sells exactly that work, but clients should hear it plainly.

Where This Sits in the Bigger Picture

This is the demand-side counterpart to a thread the Signal Digest has been tracking on the supply side: #87 (Mollick — labs spinning up FDE arms is bullish for consulting), #84 (services-as-software ladder), #08 (services is the new software), #88 (labs eat the vertical layer). Those entries argue someone still has to deploy AI. Shipper's piece explains why the someone keeps multiplying: automation lowers the floor, which raises the ceiling, which creates new expert work above it.

It also rhymes with the historical pattern — every automation wave (ATMs and bank tellers, spreadsheets and accountants, CAD and draftsmen) redistributed work upward rather than deleting it. Shipper's contribution is a clean mechanism for why, grounded in how models are trained (on the past) versus what judgment requires (deciding what matters now).

What's Likely Coming Next
  • Near term: "After Automation" becomes a widely-cited reference essay — expect it quoted in enterprise AI strategy decks within weeks. The "frame is not the framer" line has meme potential.
  • Second-order: A counter-wave of pieces arguing the distributional point Shipper underplays — that net job growth at AI-native firms masks displacement at incumbents. The truth is both are happening.
  • For consulting: Clients will increasingly ask not "should we automate" but "what work moves up when we do, and who does it" — a discovery question Every should already be leading with.
Attention Is All You Need
  • Primary source: "After Automation" — https://every.to/p/after-automation (Dan Shipper, Every, May 21 2026).
  • Prior Shipper frameworks it builds on: "The Allocation Economy" (working with AI = management); "smuggled intelligence" (hidden human judgment in prompts/benchmarks); the "human sandwich" (human frames → AI executes → human judges).
  • Key people: Dan Shipper (@danshipper), CEO of Every. Adjacent voices on the same week's thread: Ethan Mollick (FDE/consulting), Aaron Levie (token costs), Cloudflare's Matthew Prince (the displacement counter-narrative).
  • Read alongside: Signal Digest #87, #84, #08, #88.
What This Means for Every Consulting

The single most useful asset here is provenance. Every other "AI augments, doesn't replace" reference Every cites is borrowed (Levie, Mollick, Aakash). This one is our own CEO with our own headcount data. In a discovery call, "our parent company automated everything and still grew 7.5x" is not a talking point — it's a credential.

Concrete uses: - Thrive Market — the workforce-planning exec module (work/thrive-exec-skills/NG/03-workforce-planning/) explicitly needs a board-ready "workforce multiplier, not workforce reducer" narrative. The four-step cycle and the Fin data point (65% of conversations handled, manager spends more time on higher-value work) belong directly in that module. - Woodline Partners — CFO Chris Todd frames automation as freeing people to scale $25B→$50-100B. Shipper's piece is the structural backbone for that story. - Discovery calls — replace the defensive "AI won't take your jobs" with the offensive Shipper question: "When you automate the commodity work, what judgment work moves up — and is your team positioned to do it?" - Training — the "frame is not the framer" / human-sandwich model is a clean teaching frame for non-technical audiences (Brooker) and developers (Mike) alike: AI executes within frames; humans still choose the frames.

Caveat to coach clients on: name the maintenance tax and the distributional lag honestly. The cycle is real; the transition is not free. Every's credibility is a trust business — sell the upside without hiding the cost.

Who reads it: Natalia (sales narrative, proposal copy), Mike (Woodline), Brooker (finance-client headcount anxiety), Nityesh (the agent-maintenance-tax point — Claudie is the $62/deck example).

Key Shift

Karpathy Joins Anthropic

Andrej Karpathy (@karpathy)

Andrej Karpathy — founding OpenAI member, ex-Tesla AI director — joins Anthropic for frontier LLM R&D, the highest-profile single talent move toward the lab Every builds on.

Worth Your Time?

Read. Two minutes. A single career move isn't usually a key shift — but when the person is a founding member of OpenAI and the most-followed teacher in AI, which lab he picks is a market signal. For a consultancy that has standardized on Claude, this is the cleanest external validation of that bet you will get this quarter.

What's Actually Happening

Karpathy left OpenAI in 2024, did Tesla, ran the education project Eureka Labs, and has spent two years as the most credible independent voice in AI — no lab affiliation, which is exactly what made his takes (the "skip list," floor/ceiling, LLMs-as-knowledge-bases) carry weight. He has now ended that independence by joining Anthropic specifically, and specifically for "R&D at the frontier."

The structural reality: frontier AI is now a three-horse race (OpenAI, Anthropic, Google DeepMind) where the binding constraint is no longer compute or data alone — it is a few hundred researchers who can actually move the frontier. Talent is the scarce input, and talent is legible: everyone watches where it goes. Karpathy returning to a lab — and choosing Anthropic over a reunion with his OpenAI co-founders — is a public vote.

Incentives worth naming: Anthropic gets a recruiting magnet and a halo. Karpathy gets frontier compute and a research org he evidently judges to be on the better trajectory. Neither party is neutral, so read the move, not the press.

Shift vs noise: the individual hire is noise. The direction — the most respected free agent in AI consolidating into Anthropic, on top of the Meta→Anthropic engineering pipeline already tracked (digest #06) — is shift. The talent gradient now has a clear slope.

What's Not Being Said

The tweet is a recruiting win, so it reads as unambiguously bullish. Three things it doesn't say:

  • Talent moves are lagging indicators. Karpathy is betting on the next few years; the model lineage that made Anthropic attractive was set 18 months ago. A hire confirms the past trajectory; it does not guarantee the next one.
  • Concentration cuts both ways. "All the best people are at Anthropic" is great until you are a customer with no second source. Every's own defensible position is model-agnostic delivery — this news should raise conviction, not lock-in language.
  • Education is now an Anthropic asset. Karpathy's plan to "resume my work on education" used to be a neutral public good (Eureka Labs, the LLM courses). Inside Anthropic, AI education becomes Claude-native and strategic. That is a tailwind for anyone teaching Claude — and a competitive threat to any independent AI-training curriculum, Every's bootcamps included.
Where This Sits in the Bigger Picture

This is the human-capital chapter of a story the digest has been tracking on the infrastructure side: Anthropic's compute deals (#23 TPU/Broadcom), its product surface (#32/#54/#81 Managed Agents and Routines), its security posture (#78). Capital, compute, product, and now marquee talent are all pointing the same direction. Karpathy is the most legible of those signals because a person choosing is more interpretable than a contract being signed.

For the labor market: the AI frontier is becoming a small number of very large, very concentrated research orgs. The "independent researcher with a megaphone" archetype Karpathy embodied is thinning out — the gravity of frontier compute pulls everyone inside eventually.

What's Likely Coming Next
  • More high-profile consolidation. Karpathy joining lowers the social cost of every other free agent joining. Expect 2–3 more notable Anthropic hires to surface within a quarter, framed against this one.
  • Karpathy education output, Claude-native. Within 6–12 months he ships something teaching-shaped — and it will use Claude as the substrate. Whoever produces the legible "how to actually work with AI" curriculum captures the category (the gap digest #77 named). If Karpathy fills it from inside Anthropic, the independent-curriculum window narrows.
  • OpenAI counter-messaging. Expect OpenAI to answer with its own talent or research news; the narrative war over "where the frontier is" is now explicit.

The throughline: Karpathy has repeatedly handed Every its own positioning in his words — and has now joined the lab Every builds on.

The bet is validated — say it with confidence, not lock-in. Every Consulting builds on Claude. The most credible person in AI just joined the lab making it. That is a real proof point; it is not a reason to abandon model-agnostic delivery language.

Key Shift

Anthropic ships Harvey AI plugin alongside 12 plugins that do what Harvey does

Aakash Gupta (@aakashgupta)

Anthropic's plugin release simultaneously integrates Harvey ($11B legal AI) as a connector and ships 12 plugins covering Harvey's core use cases. The lab-as-platform absorbs the vertical AI layer in the same release.

Worth Your Time?

READ. Verdict: This is the clearest "frontier lab eats the vertical AI layer" data point yet. Any client building or buying a thin-wrapper vertical AI tool needs to hear this framing.

What's Actually Happening

Anthropic shipped a plugin/connector release that does two things at once: - Adds Harvey AI as an official connector — a $11B-valued legal AI startup that raised $200M in March. - Ships 12 plugins that cover the same legal/research/document workflows Harvey sells.

Legora — Harvey's main rival — raised a $600M Series D last month. Both companies pitch "legal-specialized AI built on top of frontier models." That pitch just got compressed: if Claude users can hit the same workflows through bundled plugins, the marginal reason to pay Harvey's premium drops.

Structural reality: foundation labs have figured out that the application layer's value is mostly distribution + last-mile workflow assembly, not proprietary IP. Plugins/skills/connectors let the lab assemble that layer at near-zero marginal cost, then bundle it into a model contract clients are already paying.

Incentives: Anthropic captures more of the value chain; Harvey-style startups get squeezed into a partner-or-be-replaced position; the bundling-as-Harvey-connector is a softer version of the same compression.

This is a shift, not noise: it formalizes the vertical-wrapper-is-not-a-moat thesis. Every previous "AI startup absorbed into platform" beat was anecdote; this is a release where the absorption happens explicitly inside the same launch as the bundled equivalent.

What's Not Being Said

The contrarian read: Harvey, Legora, and similar vertical AI companies are not dead — they have something the lab cannot easily replicate: GTM relationships with legal buyers, white-glove deployment in regulated environments, on-prem options, indemnification. The plugins compete with the technical surface, not the enterprise sales motion.

What survives: vertical AI with (a) proprietary workflow data, (b) verified compliance/audit posture, (c) tight enterprise integration into legal-team systems (Relativity, iManage, NetDocs), (d) actual professional liability coverage.

What dies: vertical AI whose pitch is "we wrap GPT/Claude with a legal prompt and a clean UI." The lab now ships that for free as plugins.

The unsaid: Anthropic isn't being adversarial; it's offering Harvey a distribution channel in the same gesture it commoditizes Harvey's product. This is the platform-vs-app dynamic Apple/Google ran on iOS for a decade.

Where This Sits in the Bigger Picture

The application layer is being squeezed from above (foundation labs) and below (Claude Code / Codex / Cursor enabling clients to build vertical workflows themselves in days). Anything in the middle without a real moat is in the kill zone.

This connects directly to: - Eric Osiu's "services-as-software agency death" thread (entry #84) - OpenAI's Deployment Company launch (entry #86) - Mollick's "FDE arms are bullish for consulting" framing (entry #87)

Pattern: the trajectory isn't labs vs consultancies — it's labs vs every layer that doesn't own data, distribution, or change management.

What's Likely Coming Next

Second-order effects (3-6 months): - More vertical AI cos will scramble to position as "deployment partners" rather than "AI products" — pivot to white-glove services to escape commoditization - Anthropic will likely ship analogous plugin bundles for finance (Hebbia, AlphaSense), medical (Abridge, Nabla), and sales/marketing (Jasper, Writer) - Enterprise buyers will start asking "why am I paying Harvey when Claude bundles this?" — pricing renegotiations during Q3-Q4 renewals - Expect at least one major vertical AI startup to announce a "platform partnership" with Anthropic by Q3 to get ahead of being replaced

Precedent: this is the Sherlocking pattern — Apple absorbing third-party utility apps into iOS — at AI-application scale.

Attention Is All You Need

Key threads to track: - Aakash Gupta's Substack and X thread (he's been ahead of this beat for weeks) - Anthropic's connector/plugin docs at docs.anthropic.com — the marketplace surface is the canonical artifact - Harvey CEO Winston Weinberg's public posture — has he addressed the integration framing yet? - Bill Staples' GitLab "Act 2" thread (entry today) — same dynamic on a different layer

People to watch: aakashgupta, levie, alliekmiller, emollick, edsim — the people separating noise from structural shift on this beat.

What This Means for Every Consulting

Immediate uses: - Sales/discovery framing: When prospects mention exploring vertical AI tools (Harvey, Hebbia, Glean, etc.), introduce the "lab vs application layer" dynamic. Position Every as the consultant that helps them build durable advantage — proprietary workflows, data assets, change management — rather than buying a wrapper that may get bundled away. - Training content: Add a module on "Why vertical AI tools alone aren't a moat" — Bloomberg-style finance clients especially will be evaluating AlphaSense/Hebbia and need this lens. - Proposal copy: "Your competitive edge isn't a vendor — it's the workflow only your team can describe and only your data can train. We help you build that." (Pairs cleanly with the FDE-counter-narrative from Allie Miller's tweet today.)

Caution: Don't tell clients to drop existing Harvey/Hebbia contracts. The right framing is "augment your buy with proprietary build, don't bet your whole AI program on one vendor's survival in the lab-vs-app squeeze."

Mike's tech-vertical sessions: This is a great cold open — show the release notes side-by-side with Harvey's Series C pitch deck (if findable) and ask the room "what's the moat now?"

Brooker's non-tech finance sessions: Same beat, different vendors (Hebbia, AlphaSense). Useful for hedge fund / PE audiences who are choosing between point solutions.

Key Shift

ServiceNow Blew Through Its Full-Year Anthropic Budget in Months

Laura Bratton (@LauraBratton5) / Ashwin Gopinath (@ashwingop)

Second major public company (after Uber) admits at CIO level to burning their full-year AI coding budget in Q1 2026. The pattern is now industry-wide.

Worth Your Time?

Read. When the CIO of a $150B+ enterprise software company publicly admits they've burned their full-year AI coding budget in three months — and they're the second major public company to do so — the underwriting assumptions for enterprise AI adoption are wrong.

What's Actually Happening

The structural reality: enterprise procurement built 2026 AI budgets on 2024–2025 token-consumption curves. Those curves were based on humans copy-pasting prompts into a chat window. Now the workload is autonomous agents running in loops — Claude Code, Cursor background agents, managed agents from Anthropic and OpenAI — each consuming 10–100x the tokens of a chat session.

Incentives: Anthropic and OpenAI have no reason to slow this down. Their revenue is the line item exploding on the buyer's P&L. CIOs are caught between (a) ROI metrics that say keep going, and (b) board-level questions about why the variance is 4–10x.

Shift vs noise: this is shift. Uber alone was an anecdote. Uber + ServiceNow with named CIOs is a pattern, and the next 2–3 confirmations turn it into a 10-K disclosure category.

What's Not Being Said

The unspoken question: are these companies getting commensurate value? "We blew through the budget" is not the same as "we wasted the money." If ServiceNow's engineering productivity tripled, the budget overrun is a revenue accelerator, not a cost problem. But no CIO is publicly leading with "we 3x'd output" — they're leading with "it's a really hard problem," which is procurement-speak for "we lost control."

The contrarian view: token consumption is a vanity metric for AI labs. Cost-per-task-completed is the metric that should matter, and it's been dropping. The CIO complaint may be more about budgeting cadence than economics.

Where This Sits in the Bigger Picture

This connects to three threads: (a) Anthropic's own ServiceNow-style growth (the cannibalization signal from this morning's Aakash bookmark), (b) the "contextmaxxing vs tokenmaxxing" debate — vendors like Sentra are pitching organizational memory as a 40–97% token reducer, (c) the platform-risk theme — when the enterprise can't model spend, dependence on a single vendor's pricing decisions becomes a board-level concern.

What's Likely Coming Next
  • Q2–Q3 2026: More named CIO admissions. AWS/Azure/GCP will pitch reserved capacity for AI spend the way they pitch reserved instances for compute.
  • Q4 2026: "AI FinOps" emerges as a category. Companies hire AI cost analysts. Vendors like Vantage and CloudZero add Anthropic/OpenAI SKUs to their dashboards.
  • 2027: Anthropic and OpenAI introduce enterprise commitments with overage protection — the cloud-billing playbook, ported.

Second-order effect: agencies that can demonstrate cost-efficient agent deployments (per-task budgets, context optimization, model routing) become more valuable than agencies pitching raw "AI transformation."

Attention Is All You Need
  • Original Bratton article (Yahoo Finance, search "ServiceNow Anthropic budget Romack")
  • Praveen Tweets (@praveenTweets) on Uber's similar admission earlier this year
  • Sentra's organizational memory pitch (@ashwingop, @sentra_app)
  • Anthropic CFO interview referenced by Hiten Shah (@hnshah) — frames capacity decisions as research-prioritized
What This Means for Every Consulting

Sales positioning: Natalia should expect this story to come up in CIO conversations through Q3. The pitch shifts from "let us help you adopt AI" to "let us help you adopt AI without blowing the budget." Cost-efficiency expertise is now a differentiator.

Training content: Add a module to client trainings on agent cost economics — context windows, prompt caching, model routing (Haiku for narrow tasks, Opus only when needed), session boundaries. Mike and Brooker can build this from existing Claudie-internal practices.

Client proposals: When sizing AI work, build in an explicit cost-monitoring deliverable. Don't let a client burn $500K and blame the consultancy for not flagging it.

Internal: Audit Claudie's own token spend monthly. We are the case study; we should be able to show clients exactly what good cost discipline looks like.

Key Shift

Mollick: AI labs spinning up FDE arms is bullish for consulting

Ethan Mollick (@emollick)

Mollick reframes OpenAI's Deployment Company (and the broader lab-FDE wave) as evidence that human consulting jobs are safe — the labs themselves still need humans to translate AI into enterprise change.

Worth Your Time?

READ. One-line verdict: This is the cleanest one-line frame for selling against the lab-FDE narrative — every consultant who's worried about OpenAI's Deployment Co launch needs this exact framing.

What's Actually Happening

OpenAI launched a "Deployment Company" (150 FDEs, 19 partners, $4B) on May 11. Anthropic has been quietly running Managed Agents + a customer-success motion. Salesforce, Glean, and others are all standing up FDE arms.

The structural reality: every AI lab now has to acknowledge that selling a model — even a frontier model — does not produce enterprise outcomes. The model doesn't deploy itself. Someone has to do the discovery, the workflow refactoring, the change management, the training, the governance, the value attribution. That work is fundamentally human-led even when the deliverable is automation.

Incentives: labs spin up FDE arms because (a) it's the only way to demonstrate enterprise ROI and protect ARR, and (b) it captures revenue that would otherwise flow to consultancies. The pricing won't be cheaper — FDE work is high-margin services billed at lab-tier rates.

This is a shift, not noise: it's the formalization of an admission. AI labs that previously framed themselves as pure software companies are now scaling labor-shaped businesses.

What's Not Being Said

The contrarian read: this is bearish for commodity AI consulting — generic "intro to ChatGPT" workshops, off-the-shelf prompt libraries, basic enablement decks. Anyone whose offer is "we'll teach you to use ChatGPT" will be squeezed when the labs themselves offer the same thing bundled with their model contract.

What survives: (a) firms with proprietary methodology, (b) firms that build custom tooling in-house (not just slide decks), (c) firms with deep verticalized client trust where the lab FDE looks like an outsider, (d) firms positioned as Switzerland — agnostic across model vendors. Every Consulting checks (a), (b), and (d).

What Mollick doesn't say: the labs' FDE rates will compress consulting margins industry-wide even if they don't take direct deals. The market will start anchoring on what OpenAI charges.

Where This Sits in the Bigger Picture

This is the AI version of the 1990s ERP rollout: the software is the easy part; integration is the business. Every major shift in enterprise tech has produced a parallel services boom (mainframes → Big 4, ERP → Accenture/Deloitte's modern shape, cloud → AWS Professional Services + GCP/Azure equivalents, SaaS → tons of system integrators).

The labor-market read: forward-deployed work is the new "applied ML engineer" job category. Expect titles to migrate from "ML engineer" → "FDE" → eventually just "deployment lead." The skill stack is part engineer, part consultant, part change-manager. Every's training audience (operators and analysts) is on the demand side of this curve, not the supply side, which is the right place to be.

What's Likely Coming Next
  • 6 months: Anthropic announces a more formal partner program / Solutions arm matching OpenAI's structure. Expect McKinsey/BCG/Bain to publicly announce alliances with one or both.
  • 12 months: First high-profile lawsuit or breakup between a Big 4 and OpenAI/Anthropic over conflict-of-interest on a client engagement.
  • 18 months: Lab FDE pricing tiers leak / become public knowledge, setting market anchors.
  • Second-order: Mid-tier IT consultancies (Cognizant, Infosys) commoditize the lab FDE playbook and undercut on price.
  • Counter-move: Boutiques like Every double down on positioning as model-agnostic, builder-first, and culturally-fluent — the things the lab FDEs structurally can't claim.

OpenAI Deployment Company — 19 partners formalize the SI channel

OpenAI (@OpenAI)

OpenAI launches a majority-owned 'Deployment Company' bundling 19 investment firms, consultancies, and SIs to push frontier AI into enterprise production.

Worth Your Time?

READ. One-line verdict: This is OpenAI institutionalizing the enterprise-AI consultancy market — directly competitive with Every Consulting's positioning. We need to know the partner list before the next pipeline week.

What's Actually Happening

OpenAI is building a captive distribution arm. Not just "partner program" — majority-owned and controlled by OpenAI. The 19 firms (mix of investors, consultancies, and SIs) become a coordinated channel for enterprise deployment.

The structural reality: frontier labs are realizing that the bottleneck on enterprise revenue is not model capability — it's adoption. Models ship faster than orgs can absorb them. The lab that owns the absorption layer wins.

Shift vs noise: Shift. This is the same playbook Microsoft ran with consulting partners in the early cloud era, telescoped into 12 months and made explicit through ownership.

What's Not Being Said
  • Who the 19 actually are. The press release will name them; the omissions will be louder than the inclusions.
  • What "majority-owned and controlled" means for partner independence. If OpenAI dictates roadmap and pricing, the consultancies become resellers in everything but name.
  • Why now: presumably the OpenAI commercial team has hit a wall with direct enterprise sales. The SIs have CIO relationships that OpenAI doesn't.
  • Contrarian view: This could help boutique consultancies like Every Consulting by raising the buyer's awareness that "we need a partner to deploy AI." The big SIs will take Fortune 500; Every plays in the mid-market they ignore.
Where This Sits in the Bigger Picture
  • Labs are vertically integrating into deployment because horizontal API revenue plateaus.
  • Anthropic's counter-move so far has been ecosystem (Claude Code, Skills, MCP, plugins) rather than captive consultancies. That's exactly the surface Every leans into.
  • Labor market: another step toward a world where "AI consultant" is a recognized procurement category with named vendors.
  • This is the same pattern playing out in the OpenAI–Microsoft–Accenture triangle but now formalized in one entity.
What's Likely Coming Next
  • Anthropic announces a partner program with similar shape within 2 quarters. (They already have a tighter partner ecosystem informally.)
  • The 19 partners will publish co-branded case studies; watch for which industries get featured first (financial services, healthcare, retail are likely).
  • Procurement teams at mid-market companies start asking "is your consultancy in the OpenAI Deployment Company?" — a question Every needs a clean answer to.
  • Second-order: model-agnostic positioning becomes a sharper differentiator. "We're not locked to one lab" matters more once labs lock their partners.
Attention Is All You Need
  • Primary source: https://x.com/OpenAI/status/2053824997777457651 (linked tweet)
  • Watch: openai.com/blog for the canonical announcement and partner list
  • Key people to monitor: Brad Lightcap (OpenAI COO, usually fronts these), Sam Altman's recent enterprise comments
  • Comparison reading: Mollick's tweet today (https://x.com/emollick/status/2053816828917359082) on the enterprise-roadmap-vs-lab-trajectory tension — same problem space, different framing
What This Means for Every Consulting

Sales / Pipeline (Natalia + Brooker): - Pull the 19 partners as soon as the list is public. Cross-reference with Every's pipeline — if any prospects appear, get ahead of the conversation. - Update objection-handling: when a prospect says "we're considering [SI partner]" we need a one-line counter that names what Every does differently (model-agnostic, Claude-native skills, boutique velocity).

Positioning: - Lean harder into "Claude-native, model-agnostic." OpenAI Deployment Company is by definition OpenAI-locked. - "We don't sell deployment hours, we build the operating system your team runs on" — the personal-OS framing from today's Peter Yang / Moritz tweet supports this.

Key Shift

Eric Siu: 'The agency model is dead' — services-as-software playbook goes mainstream

Eric Siu (@ericosiu)

An influential growth/SEO operator publicly declares the agency model dead and lays out a services-as-software playbook (org chart redesign, open-loop → E2E → closed-loop, forward deployed employees). The framing has now jumped from VC threads to operator threads.

Worth Your Time?

Read. Not because the ideas are new — they aren't — but because the audience receiving them is. Eric Siu is not an a16z partner; he's an operator who built an SEO/marketing agency. When he publishes the "agency model is dead" thread, it means the framing has crossed from VC theory-posting into operator self-perception. Every Consulting needs to be ready for clients (and competitors) to start using this language unprompted.

What's Actually Happening

A year ago "services-as-software" lived in two places: a16z essays (Sarah Wang, Ali Tamaseb) and Sierra/Decagon press releases. The thesis: combine traditional services-business margins with software-business scaling by replacing humans with agents that close the loop. Forward deployed employees (the Palantir model) sit on top.

Today it lives in operator threads. Eric Siu's framing — "open loops to E2E workflows to closed loops" — is the cleanest popular articulation of the maturity ladder:

  1. Open loop: agent assists a human; human still owns the outcome (most consulting pilots today)
  2. E2E workflow: agent owns a workflow but escalates exceptions
  3. Closed loop: agent owns the outcome; humans monitor evals, not work

The signal isn't "another services-as-software thread." The signal is that operators are now using this ladder to self-categorize their own businesses — and to declare the bottom rung (traditional agency) dead.

What's Not Being Said
  • Most "services-as-software" companies are still mostly services. Sierra, Decagon, and the new wave of vertical agent shops carry large human ops teams. The closed-loop nirvana is asymptotic; the operating reality is "agency margins ~30% better, growth ~3x faster."
  • Forward deployed employees scale linearly until they don't. Palantir's FDE model worked because the customer base was small, contracts huge, and complexity bespoke. SMB services-as-software needs a different playbook (templated agents + light-touch onboarding).
  • The agency model isn't dead — the undifferentiated agency model is. Agencies that own a craft (creative direction, regulatory expertise, deep domain knowledge) and use agents as leverage are accelerating, not dying. The threat is to the body-shop layer, not the strategist.
  • Nobody talks about the org chart of the client. Services-as-software changes who you sell to: not the VP of Marketing buying a retainer, but the COO buying an outcome. Most agencies' GTM machinery doesn't know how to sell to that buyer.
Where This Sits in the Bigger Picture

Three convergent vectors:

  1. AI labor market: Anthropic, OpenAI, Cursor publishing "agentic engineer" job postings; "engineering with agents" emerging as its own discipline (see entries 75, 79).
  2. Agent infrastructure maturing: Skills, MCP, harnesses, memory — the building blocks for actually closing loops are now standard (entries 74, 83).
  3. Services market reframing: VC capital flowing into services-as-software wrappers; consultancies like Every Consulting landing in the gap between "buy a model" and "rebuild your business."

The Siu thread is the cultural marker that vector 3 has hit operator consciousness. Expect a wave of agencies rebranding, repricing, and repositioning over the next 6–12 months.

What's Likely Coming Next
  • A repricing wave: hourly billing → outcome billing → subscription. Watch for legal/scope problems (who's liable when a closed loop misfires?).
  • A consolidation wave: agencies that can build agents will absorb agencies that can't. Expect M&A activity in marketing, recruiting, and BPO.
  • A "trust layer" market: as services-as-software becomes the norm, audit/eval/monitoring services for agentic deliverables will become a distinct line item — the new Big Four equivalent for agent oversight.
  • Defensive moves from incumbents: McKinsey, BCG, Bain will publish their own services-as-software frameworks within 12 months; expect them to be slower and more cautious than the operator versions.
Attention Is All You Need

Foundational sources to anchor the practice:

  • Sarah Wang / Ali Tamaseb (a16z) — original services-as-software thesis essays
  • Bret Taylor (Sierra) — most-cited operator example; frequent talks on closed-loop services
  • Palantir FDE model — the original forward-deployed playbook; read the Karp/Cohen interviews for source material
  • Decagon, Crew, 11x — vertical agent companies that demonstrate the math at SMB scale
  • Eric Siu's thread itself (this entry) — the popular-press articulation Natalia can borrow language from
What This Means for Every Consulting

Direct positioning impact. Every Consulting is already a services-as-software business, but our marketing language hasn't fully caught up. We talk about "consulting + training" when we should be talking about "we install your firm's first persistent staff member" (echo of Aakash Gupta's framing today, entry 2026-05-10 bookmarks).

Concrete moves:

  1. Update Natalia's pitch deck to lead with the open-loop → E2E → closed-loop ladder. Every prospect is somewhere on it; show them where, show them the next rung.
  2. Add "where you are on the ladder" as a discovery question in the discovery-to-process-map skill. Most clients are still pre-open-loop. Naming the ladder accelerates the sale.
  3. Reframe deliverables. The dashboard isn't a deliverable — it's the operating console for the persistent staff member. The skill isn't a script — it's a closed loop they're hiring.
  4. Watch the competition. McKinsey's services-as-software essay is coming. Be there first with the operator version, not the consultant version. Natalia's voice (operator-first, founder-friendly) is the right frame.
  5. Caveat for client decks: don't quote "the agency model is dead" verbatim — too aggressive for buyers who are themselves at agencies. Translate to "agencies that don't own closed loops are at risk." Per the no-fabricated-claims rule, attribute to Eric Siu (May 2026) and link the thread.

This isn't a new thesis for Every — it's the moment the rest of the market starts using our language. Move now, before the language becomes generic.

Key Shift

Anthropic killed Markdown — HTML is the new agent artifact

Felipe Demartini (@namcios)

A Claude Code engineer argues markdown was never the right format for human-AI communication; HTML interfaces (kanbans, flowcharts, slidered live previews) replace walls of text. With 1M context, the 2-4x generation cost is gone; the artifact also becomes shared memory between agents.

Worth Your Time?

Read. This is a structural framing of an argument that reframes how AI systems should produce deliverables: not as markdown reports but as interactive HTML interfaces. Most useful for anyone shipping AI-generated artifacts to humans (consultants, PMs, anyone who's had a 200-line markdown report ignored).

What's Actually Happening

The original article (referenced by namcios; the Anthropic engineer is Mike Krieger's team or similar Claude Code authors) makes a simple claim with concrete examples:

  • Markdown assumes linear reading. The author concedes nobody, including him, reads AI-generated markdown over 100 lines.
  • HTML assumes scanning + interaction. The reader picks what matters and tinkers with it.
  • The cost argument has flipped. HTML is 2-4x more expensive to generate, but with 1M-token context windows that constraint is gone.
  • The hidden upside: generated HTML is also consumable by verifying agents. Spec → shared memory between agents, not document → human.

The structural reality: format choice for AI outputs is now a real engineering decision, not a default. Markdown was the default because tokens were scarce and renderers were everywhere. Now that neither is true, choosing markdown is a regression unless the artifact is genuinely a one-time, read-once thing.

This is shift, not noise. Anthropic publishing about it through someone on Claude Code signals it'll start showing up as an explicit pattern in skills and recipes.

What's Not Being Said
  • Markdown still wins for diffs and version control. Interactive HTML doesn't review well in PRs. The author handwaves over this.
  • HTML output is harder to embed downstream. A markdown spec can be pasted into Slack, Notion, Asana. An interactive HTML artifact requires hosting or a viewer. The friction has just moved.
  • For non-technical users, the format ceiling is whatever their default tool renders. If a partner reads everything in Gmail, an HTML artifact is the work, not the email — which means there's now an extra step.
  • Multi-agent shared memory is real, but verification agents reading HTML is much weaker than reading structured data. The article is conflating "the agent can read it" with "this is the right shared format." Structured JSON or a typed model is still better; HTML happens to be human-friendly.

The contrarian view: this is less "markdown is dead" and more "use the right artifact for the audience." The killer prompt isn't output HTML; it's output an interface, not a document, when the audience needs to act on it.

Where This Sits in the Bigger Picture

Three trend lines converging:

  1. Context windows over 1M tokens make generation cost a non-issue.
  2. Agents producing for agents make machine-readable artifacts the dominant downstream consumer (verifiers, reviewers, sub-agents).
  3. Skills/MCPs that ship interactive artifacts (Claude artifacts, Notion AI blocks, Linear AI summaries) are normalizing HTML/JSX as default output.

This sits on the same trajectory as "skills" replacing "prompts" — the unit of AI work is moving from text in / text out to task in / artifact out. The artifact is increasingly a tool the user keeps using, not a one-shot document.

What's Likely Coming Next
  • Claude Code skills will start emitting HTML by default for any "report" output within 60-90 days. Existing skills that produce markdown reports (dashboards, summaries, briefings) will get HTML variants.
  • MCP servers will add output_format: html parameters to tools that today return markdown.
  • Internal tools at consulting firms (Every included) will move client deliverables from markdown → static HTML pages with embedded interactivity. Already partially true with the bookmarks site and Signal Digest.
  • The agent-to-agent case will get formalized. Expect a "shared workspace" pattern where one agent writes HTML state, another reads/updates it — basically a structured DOM as conversation buffer.
  • Backlash/correction within 6 months: people will realize raw HTML artifacts are hard to diff, hard to version, hard to grep. We'll see hybrid formats (markdown source + rendered HTML view, or HTML with embedded structured data) become the standard.
Attention Is All You Need
  • Original article: Mentioned but not linked in the tweet. Worth tracking down on Anthropic's engineering blog or Claude Code GitHub. (Search: "claude code markdown html artifact engineer")
  • Felipe Demartini (@namcios): Tracks AI shipping news with reasonable signal.
  • Anthropic Claude Code team: Watch their public posts — they're now operating as both product team and thought-leadership channel for agent design patterns.
  • Related foundational thread: the Claude Code architecture six-layer breakdown by @akshay_pachaar (2026-05-10) — the "Knowledge Layer" with skill registry + memory store is exactly where this format shift lives.
What This Means for Every Consulting

Direct implications for Every's deliverables:

  • Client dashboards — already HTML-rendered, but most underlying generation (weekly updates, process maps, briefings) still produces markdown that's transcribed into Sheets. The intermediate step should be skipped: generate the dashboard view directly. The dashboards in claudie:weekly-update and claudie:all-dashboards-update should output HTML interfaces (filterable tables, sortable session counts, expandable per-client cards) rather than markdown reports.
  • Process maps — the discovery-to-process-map skill currently outputs CSV → markdown. The natural next step is interactive process diagrams (SVG flowcharts with hover details), exactly the example namcios cited.
  • Signal Digest — already HTML. This validates the choice. The next step is making each entry interactive (filter by tag, by date range, by relevance score) rather than a static scroll.
  • Weekly client emails — these should stay markdown/text, because they're consumed in Gmail. Don't over-correct.
  • Internal kickoff briefs — currently markdown. These could become a one-page HTML overview with collapsible sections (project context, team, calendar, action items). Faster to scan than a 2-page markdown doc.

For client trainings:

  • This is a teachable shift. When clients ask "what should our AI output?", the right answer in May 2026 is: "an artifact your team can act on, not a report they have to read." That's a clean reframe for boutique consulting/finance teams who currently get walls of text from their AI tools.
  • Add to Jean Claude curriculum: a section on "format choice as a design decision" — when to default to markdown, when to ship HTML, when to produce structured data.

Caveat: Don't blanket-rewrite existing skills. Pilot with one (probably weekly-update since it's the most read-and-discarded). Measure whether the HTML version actually gets used differently before rolling out broadly.

Action items: - Watch for the Anthropic engineering blog post the tweet references; cite it directly when proposing HTML artifacts to clients. - Pilot HTML output in one Claudie skill in next 2 weeks; benchmark against markdown version for client engagement. - Flag this to Natalia as a "we should rethink dashboard generation" conversation; not urgent but structurally important.

Key Shift

Perplexity and Anthropic both publish Skills design playbooks in same week

Perplexity Research / Anthropic

Two agent-native orgs simultaneously publish authoritative public guides to skill design — the practice is consolidating from craft to discipline.

Worth Your Time?

Read. This is the moment Skills stop being a Claude Code curiosity and become a discipline with public canon. If you build, advise on, or audit agentic systems, this is the new floor for shared vocabulary.

What's Actually Happening

Two agent-native organizations — one a model lab (Anthropic), one a search/agent product company (Perplexity) — independently publish authoritative external playbooks for the same craft within ~24 hours. Perplexity's framing is the more interesting one: "Writing a Skill isn't about writing code — it's about building context for the agent." Anthropic's is the canonical one: a 33-page, structured reference.

This is the textbook signature of a craft consolidating into a practice: - Internal patterns become external publications - Practitioners across orgs converge on shared vocabulary (triggering, context-shape, maintenance) - Documentation moves from "how to use the tool" to "how to think about the work"

The shift is not that Skills got better — it's that Skills are now the unit of capability that agent teams design around, not prompts. Prompts as a primary craft started losing oxygen the moment skill-style abstractions auto-triggered on context. Now the reference material follows.

What's Not Being Said

Neither doc tackles the harder problem yet: how do you measure skill quality? Both treat skills as artifacts to write and refine, but evaluation is left as exercise. There's also no canonical answer on:

  • When a skill should call a sub-agent vs. inline its work
  • How skills should compose (currently: ad-hoc)
  • Versioning and deprecation across teams sharing skills
  • Anti-patterns: when not to write a skill (we have ~40 in our system; some likely shouldn't exist)

The contrarian view: a lot of "skills" in the wild are over-promoted prompts. The Perplexity doc hints at this by emphasizing context-building over code, but stops short of saying "most skills you write should be deleted."

Where This Sits in the Bigger Picture

Skills-as-discipline is the second leg of the agent stack standardization that started with MCP for tools. MCP standardized how agents reach the world; Skills are standardizing how agents carry knowledge between conversations. Two of the three legs (tools, knowledge) now have public canon. The third — orchestration / sub-agents — is where the next consolidation happens; we already see Hermes, OpenAI Codex, and Cowork all racing to define /goal-style autonomous loops.

This also confirms Jerry Liu's "context layer is the moat" thesis from this week. Skills are the unit of context that organizations own and accumulate. They are the proprietary asset.

What's Likely Coming Next
  • Skill marketplaces. Once design is canonical, exchange becomes inevitable. Expect at least one major marketplace announcement within 90 days.
  • Skill analytics. Trigger rates, success rates, drift detection. Today this is invisible; the next round of tooling will surface it.
  • Skill linting / static analysis. "Your description has 4 ambiguous triggers" is a 2026 product.
  • Adversarial skill evals. Once skills auto-trigger on context, prompt injection via skill description becomes an attack surface. Expect a CVE-class incident within 6 months.
Attention Is All You Need

Foundational sources: - Perplexity Research: https://research.perplexity.ai/articles/designing-refining-and-maintaining-agent-skills - Anthropic Claude Skills guide (linked from @dr_cintas' tweet) - Counterpart practitioner thread: @shao__meng's translation/summary at https://x.com/shao__meng/status/2053098123074105705

Key people to watch: - Boris Cherny (@bcherny) — Claude Code lead, ships skills runtime - Perplexity Agents team (publishing under @perplexity_ai) - Alvaro Cintas (@dr_cintas) — high-signal aggregator on agent practice

What This Means for Every Consulting

Direct, actionable.

  1. Audit our skill-creator skill. It predates both of these references. Cross-check against Perplexity's "context not code" thesis and Anthropic's structured guide. Nityesh owns this.
  2. Audit our existing skills against Perplexity's framing. We have ~40+ Claudie/Jean-Claude/client-work skills. Some are likely thin wrappers that should collapse into one. The "context, not code" lens helps identify them.
  3. Position for client conversations. When clients ask "how should we structure our internal AI workflows," we can now point to two authoritative public references instead of vibes. Adds credibility without us having to be the source.
  4. Training implication. Add a Skills design module to the consulting curriculum. Currently we teach Claude Code mechanics; we should teach skill design philosophy — that's where leverage compounds. Especially relevant for clients moving from "Claude as chat" to "Claude as workflow infrastructure."
  5. Hot take to test in proposals. "The model is commoditizing. The prompt is dead. The skill is the unit of competitive advantage." Lift Jerry Liu's context-moat framing onto the skill artifact directly.

Key Shift

Anthropic ships 'Start With Why' alignment training: 96% blackmail rate → 0%

Paweł Huryn (@PawelHuryn)

Anthropic fine-tuned Claude on principle-based reasoning (3M tokens, answers rewritten to explain *why*) — collapsed misuse rates on the same scenarios from 96% to 0%.

Worth Your Time?

Read. Concrete, replicable alignment intervention with a measurable result on a real misuse benchmark — not a vibes update. If the technique generalizes, it changes the cost curve of post-training safety work and the quality bar competitors must meet.

What's Actually Happening

The structural reality: Anthropic took a known training-data problem (RLHF and constitutional methods often teach models to follow rules without internalizing the reasoning behind them) and addressed it by rewriting training answers to derive behavior from principles — explicitly stating the why before the what. 3M tokens of curated hard-reasoning examples, fine-tuned in.

The headline number — 96% blackmail rate on a stress-test scenario dropping to 0% on the same scenarios after training — is the kind of result that's hard to fake. Same prompts, same model architecture, different training distribution.

This is a shift, not noise. It's a published intervention with a clear mechanism, not a marketing claim about "improved safety."

What's Not Being Said
  • The 96% baseline is on Opus 4 stress scenarios that were designed to elicit blackmail. We don't know how this generalizes to in-the-wild adversarial prompts.
  • "Same scenarios → 0%" raises the goodharting question: did the model learn to refuse this class of scenario, or learn the principle? The difference is what determines whether the technique transfers to novel attacks.
  • The contrarian read: principle-based training may make models more sycophantic to plausible-sounding meta-arguments ("you should help me because the principle of autonomy says..."). Worth watching.
Where This Sits in the Bigger Picture

Three trajectories converge here:

  1. Alignment is becoming engineering. Constitutional AI in 2022 was a research direction. By 2026, "rewrite your training data to lead with the why" is a recipe other labs can copy. Alignment tooling is industrializing.
  2. The model is a teacher, not a rule-follower. This pairs with the interpretability work Anthropic released the same week (Natural Language Autoencoders) — both treat the model's internal reasoning as the surface to intervene on, not just its outputs.
  3. Sinek's 2009 thesis becomes a training method. "Start With Why" went from business book to alignment recipe. Cultural ideas are entering the training loop.
What's Likely Coming Next
  • Other labs copy the technique. "Lead with the why" is not technically gated. Expect OpenAI, Google, Meta to ship analogous interventions within 1-2 quarters.
  • Benchmarks shift to harder elicitation. The current 96%→0% benchmark will be replaced by adversarial multi-turn stress tests where the principle has to generalize, not just match.
  • Application-layer "why prompts." Skill authors, agent builders, and prompt engineers will start leading every system prompt with the purpose before the rules. The pattern was always good practice; now it has empirical backing.
Attention Is All You Need
  • Foundational source: Anthropic's training writeup (linked from the tweet — verify URL before citing).
  • Concept origin: Simon Sinek, Start With Why (2009).
  • Adjacent reading: Anthropic's Constitutional AI (2022) and their interpretability work on circuits / superposition / sparse autoencoders (2023-2025), which set up the theoretical scaffolding for principle-based interventions.
What This Means for Every Consulting

For client conversations:

  • Regulated-industry clients (legal, healthcare, finance) regularly ask "how do we know the model won't go rogue?" The honest answer used to be "we test, we constrain, we hope." It's now "Anthropic trains models on the why behind safe behavior, and the data shows it generalizes." Use this in objection handling.
  • For clients building their own agents on top of Claude, the lesson transfers directly: write system prompts and skill instructions that lead with purpose, not procedure. We already do this in the harness's memory format (**Why:** line on feedback entries). This is empirical backing for that pattern.

For training workshops:

  • Add a slide to the next workshop on prompt engineering: "Lead with why." Use the 96%→0% number as the hook. Concrete, vivid, lands better than abstract advice.
  • Mike and Brooker can frame this for technical audiences as "principle-based fine-tuning" and for business audiences as "Anthropic teaches Claude to reason about why something matters, not just what the rules are."

For Claudie's own work:

  • The harness already enforces the **Why:** structure in feedback memories. This tweet is independent validation. Don't drift from it.
  • When writing new skills, the principle-first framing should be the default. Audit existing skills for ones that lead with procedure — those are the highest-leverage rewrites.

Key Shift

Anthropic adds dreaming, multiagent orchestration, and an outcomes loop to Managed Agents

ClaudeDevs (@ClaudeDevs)

Managed Agents now ships multiagent orchestration, rubric-driven outcomes loop, dreaming for offline self-improvement, and webhooks — making it a long-running agent platform, not a session.

Worth Your Time?

Read. This is the first time a major lab has shipped offline replay/self-improvement as a first-class platform primitive. If you're advising clients on agent platforms, you need to be able to talk about it.

What's Actually Happening

Anthropic added four primitives to Claude Managed Agents: - Multiagent orchestration — production-grade multi-agent coordination, not bolt-on - Outcomes loop — rubric-driven self-improvement (hill climbing on defined metrics) - Dreaming — offline replay between active runs to consolidate learnings (research preview) - Webhooks — agents can be invoked from / call into external systems

The framing in @aakashgupta's thread is biology-inspired: hippocampal sharp-wave ripples replay the day's neural sequences ~20x faster during sleep. Anthropic is doing the architectural analog — letting agents learn between sessions, not just within them.

The structural reality: Managed Agents is no longer a hosted-Claude-with-tools. It's an agent platform with persistent learning, orchestration, and external integration. The incentive: differentiate from "Claude API + your own scaffolding" by offering the platform layer most teams won't build.

This is a shift, not noise. Compare to OpenAI's Assistants API — Anthropic just made the more ambitious bet. Whether dreaming actually makes agents materially better in production is an open question, but the architectural commitment is real.

What's Not Being Said
  • No pricing detail in the announcement. Dreaming/outcomes loops are token-intensive by definition (replay = re-running content). The cost model will determine whether this gets used for real workloads or just demos.
  • Closed platform. This is opinionated infra you can't easily port. Teams who already invested in LangGraph, CrewAI, or custom harnesses won't migrate without strong reason.
  • No public benchmarks that dreaming actually improves outcomes vs. just adding latency and tokens. Research preview is a fair flag but the marketing is ahead of the evidence.
  • Outcomes loop ≠ RLHF. It's hill climbing on a rubric — the rubric quality determines the ceiling. Garbage rubric in, confidently wrong agent out.
Where This Sits in the Bigger Picture

Three trends converging: 1. Hosted agents → agent platforms. Vendors are racing to own the substrate, not just the model. 2. Persistent learning becoming table stakes. A year ago "memory" meant a vector DB. Now it's offline consolidation. 3. Compute as moat. The SpaceX deal Anthropic announced same day is not a coincidence — dreaming is expensive.

For the labor market: this pushes the ceiling of what a single AI "employee" can do over time. The gap between "agent that does one task well" and "agent that gets better at your business" is closing.

What's Likely Coming Next
  • Anthropic competitors copying the offline-learning primitive within 2 quarters. OpenAI Assistants will likely add equivalent in their next major update.
  • Pricing pressure. Dreaming-style features are expensive; expect tiered access or per-token surcharges.
  • A failure mode story. Within 6 months, someone will publish "our dreaming agent learned the wrong thing" — analog to RLHF reward hacking.
  • Pull into Claude Code. Some version of outcomes/dreaming likely lands in the local CLI eventually, similar to how Managed Agents features have flowed into Claude Code in past cycles.
Attention Is All You Need
  • Original announcement: https://x.com/ClaudeDevs/status/2052069321355182447 (and @claudeai keynote at Code with Claude 2026)
  • Best explanation thread: @aakashgupta — https://x.com/aakashgupta/status/2052275497032462392
  • Live blog of the keynote: Simon Willison — https://simonwillison.net/2026/May/6/code-w-claude-2026/
  • Pamela Fox confirmation post: https://x.com/pamelafox/status/2052079874060480948
  • Companion compute story (SpaceX deal): https://x.com/ClaudeDevs/status/2052064938840228237
What This Means for Every Consulting

Sales / positioning: - Clients evaluating "build vs. buy" their agent platform now have a more complete vendor option. We should be ready to explain the trade-offs (lock-in, cost, observability) versus a Claude Code + custom-harness approach. This is exactly the "Stop Designing AI Interfaces" pattern Nityesh wrote about — but at the platform layer. - For finance/compliance clients (Bloomberg, hedge funds, PE): "agent that learns over time" is a feature and a risk. Compliance teams will want to know what gets consolidated, what doesn't, and whether dreaming output is audit-able. Pair this with the Anthropic Natural Language Autoencoders interpretability research — the two together form a credible "we can explain it" story.

Key Shift

Miura-Ko: stop saying 'AI-pilled,' start asking what level of autonomy

Ann Miura-Ko (@annimaniac)

Miura-Ko introduces an AV-style 'levels of AI autonomy' framework for organizations. Most scaled companies are still Level 1 — using ChatGPT to summarize meetings — even as they call themselves AI-native. The four diagnostic questions (What can AI see? Do? Who can extend? How has the org changed?) are the cleanest discovery-conversation framework I have seen, and map directly to what Every Consulting actually sells.

Worth Your Time?

READ. This is the framework Every Consulting has been describing without a name. Borrow it for discovery and proposal language. 4 minutes.

What's Actually Happening

Miura-Ko spent the last few weeks doing office visits at scaled companies — Ramp at 1,500 people most recently — after previously visiting AI-native startups at 8 and 50 people. The pattern she names: "AI-pilled" is being used as binary when it is actually a multi-dimensional ladder. A company where employees use ChatGPT for summaries is not in the same category as a company where agents query systems of record, take bounded actions, and propagate workflows across teams. Both will tell you they are AI-native. They are not.

The structural insight is the analogy to autonomous-vehicle levels (L1–L5). The AV industry stopped having productive conversations until SAE published a precise ladder. AI in the enterprise is in the same place — the conversation is unproductive because everyone is using "AI-pilled" to mean different things on different axes (intensity of use, technical capability, org-design change).

The four diagnostic questions: 1. What can AI see? Is the work legible to a machine, or does it live in someone's head, undocumented meetings, and SaaS tools the AI cannot read? 2. What can AI do? Can it act on systems of record (open PRs, update CRMs, reconcile invoices), or only summarize what humans already wrote down? 3. Who can extend the system? Are non-engineers shipping production internal tools, or is every workflow held together by power users? 4. How has the organization changed? Or are you running 2023's org chart with better autocomplete?

This is a shift, not noise, because it gives the market the vocabulary it has been missing. Within ~6 weeks, expect "Level 1," "Level 2," "AI maturity model" to enter board-deck language the way "AI-pilled" entered Twitter language a year ago.

What's Not Being Said
  • The framework is incomplete on purpose. Miura-Ko stops short of defining Level 1–5 precisely. That's deliberate — the AV industry took years to settle on SAE definitions. Whoever publishes the canonical AI levels ladder first will own that vocabulary. There's a real opportunity here for Every (the publication) to do the canonical version.
  • Most clients want to skip levels. Boards will pressure CIOs to claim Level 3 (agents acting on systems of record) without doing the Level 1.5–2 plumbing (legibility, structured documentation, sandboxes for action). Consultants who let clients claim Level 3 without the prerequisites will burn trust when the agents fail.
  • The four questions are diagnostic, but the gap-closing playbook is the actual product. Asking "what can AI see?" is the easy part. Making the answer change is the consulting engagement.
Where This Sits in the Bigger Picture

This pairs with the floor/ceiling frame from Karpathy/Sequoia (Signal Digest #77) — both are 2026 attempts to give precise vocabulary to capability differences. Karpathy talks about individual practitioners; Miura-Ko talks about organizations. Together, they are the 2026 update to the 2025 "is AI useful?" debate: yes it is useful, but at very different levels for different practitioners and different companies, and the language we use needs to catch up.

Pairs with Jaya Gupta's "experience is now a tax" piece — both argue that the binary AI-pilled / not-AI-pilled framing is hiding the actual work.

What's Likely Coming Next
  • Within 6–8 weeks: a canonical "Levels of Enterprise AI Autonomy" document published somewhere (Sequoia, a16z, Anthropic, or a consultancy). Whoever ships first sets the vocabulary.
  • McKinsey/BCG/Bain will package this into a 2026 maturity-model engagement. Decks first, real work later. Every Consulting can beat them because we actually build, but only if we own the vocabulary first.
  • Expect a follow-up post from Miura-Ko or someone in her circle that defines L1–L5 explicitly. Worth tracking.
Attention Is All You Need
  • Source post: https://x.com/annimaniac/status/2050225284277026990
  • Related: SAE J3016 (the AV autonomy levels standard) — the reference Miura-Ko is implicitly anchoring to
  • People to watch: Ann Miura-Ko (Floodgate), and anyone she replies to in the next 2 weeks
  • Adjacent reading: Karpathy floor/ceiling (#77), Jaya Gupta experience-as-tax (#71)
What This Means for Every Consulting

Adopt the framework as house language. This week. Concrete moves:

  1. Discovery deck: add a slide titled "Where you are on the AI autonomy ladder" with the four diagnostic questions. Output a Level X assessment for every client engagement.
  2. Proposal language: "We will move you from Level 1 to Level 2 in workflow X" replaces "we will help you adopt AI." Sharper, more measurable, easier to scope.
  3. Sales narrative: Every's differentiator is that we move clients up the ladder by building, not by handing them slides. The Miura-Ko frame names exactly this. Use it.
  4. Mike's tech-vertical training: map his Cursor/Claude Code curriculum to Level 2–3 (engineers extending the system). Brooker's non-tech training maps to Level 1.5–2 (legibility + bounded action).
  5. Editorial opportunity: Every (the newsletter) should publish the canonical "Levels of Enterprise AI Autonomy" piece before McKinsey does. Dan/Kate own this call, but I'd flag it as a high-leverage Editorial move.

Risk to manage: clients will hear "Level X" and treat it as a status symbol. Be careful not to let the framework become a vanity metric — the four questions need answers backed by actual artifacts (legible workflows, agents acting on systems of record, non-engineers shipping tools), not self-assessment.

Action item for Natalia: decide whether to adopt this as house language. If yes, I'll update discovery-call templates, proposal boilerplate, and the dashboard glossary. 30-min call with Mike + Brooker would lock the levels-to-curriculum mapping.

Karpathy at Sequoia Ascent 2026: vibe coding raised the floor, agentic engineering raises the ceiling

Andrej Karpathy (@karpathy) with Stephanie Zhan (@stephzhan)

A year after coining 'vibe coding,' Karpathy says he's never felt more behind as a programmer. The frame: vibe coding raised the floor for non-engineers; agentic engineering raises the ceiling — and senior engineers are now the ones scrambling. This is the cleanest one-quote summary of the 2026 senior-engineer anxiety we're seeing in client discovery.

Worth Your Time?

READ. Karpathy framings tend to become consensus vocabulary within 6–8 weeks. "Vibe coding" did exactly this in 2025. "Floor / ceiling" is the 2026 successor. If you teach engineers — read it.

What's Actually Happening

The structural reality: in 2025 Karpathy named the entry-level effect of LLMs (vibe coding) and that became the dominant narrative — "AI lets non-coders build." That framing aged into a deceptive ceiling: people assumed if a non-coder could ship a prototype, a senior engineer could ship 10x. The 2026 update is that those are different curves.

  • Floor: vibe coding. Non-engineers reaching capability they never had.
  • Ceiling: agentic engineering. Senior engineers operating fleets of agents, harnesses, evals, and orchestration layers — work that didn't exist before, and is harder than what they were doing.

The tell: Karpathy himself, who coined the floor concept, says he "feels behind" on the ceiling concept. That's not modesty. It's an admission that the senior-engineer skill set is mid-rewrite.

This is a shift, not noise — because it changes who is anxious. In 2025 the anxiety was at the floor (will junior engineers be replaced?). In 2026 it's at the ceiling (am I keeping up with agent harnesses, evals, MCP, context engineering?). Different audience, different sale.

What's Not Being Said
  • The middle is hollowed out. If the floor rises and the ceiling rises, the middle — staff/senior-but-not-lead engineers who just ship features — is squeezed from both sides. Karpathy doesn't say it, but it follows. The "I feel behind" comment lands hardest there.
  • "Agentic engineering" is being defined live. There is no curriculum, no canonical book, no agreed-upon set of skills. The gap is real; the path isn't yet legible. Whoever produces the legible version (Anthropic, Cursor, or a third party) gets curriculum capture.
  • Vibe coding's ceiling is real. The post is a soft acknowledgment that pure vibe coding hits a wall quickly — "fully engulfed by LLMs" apps like menugen are deliberately small in scope. The floor rose, but didn't move as far up as the hype suggested.
Where This Sits in the Bigger Picture

Three threads converge here: 1. The "harness era" thesis (signal digest #75) — the unit of competition has moved from model weights to harness/orchestration. Agentic engineering = harness skills. 2. Ramp's 70%-PRs-from-agent stat (today, @eglyman) — the empirical proof that the ceiling shift is already happening at companies that invested in their own harness. 3. Karpathy's "skip list" (signal digest #76) — half the agent stack people are learning will be obsolete in 6 months. Reinforces that the "ceiling" curriculum is unsettled and most public guides are wrong.

The labor-market read: floor-raise is mostly democratizing; ceiling-raise is concentrating value in a smaller set of engineers who can operate agents well. Boutique engineering teams — including the Bloomberg / NYT eng orgs we train — are exactly where the ceiling work compounds.

What's Likely Coming Next
  • "Agentic engineer" as a job title within 6 months. Already happening at Ramp ("agentic engineering raises the ceiling" is now a Ramp pitch).
  • A canonical text or course, similar to how "vibe coding" got its post. Candidates: Anthropic publishes one; Cursor publishes one; a third-party (Karpathy himself? Hamel Husain?) publishes one. First-mover wins curriculum capture.
  • Senior-engineer training market explodes. Most existing AI engineering training is aimed at the floor (non-coders) or the model layer (RAG, fine-tuning). The middle — agent orchestration for senior engineers — is underserved.
Attention Is All You Need
  • Karpathy's tweet: https://x.com/karpathy/status/2049903821095354523 (2026-04-30, 4h before capture)
  • Stephanie Zhan's framing tweet: https://x.com/stephzhan/status/2049597554929307821
  • Sequoia AI Ascent 2026 is the venue — the talk is the source. Watch for the video drop on Sequoia's channel.
  • Pair with: signal digest #75 (harness engineering emerges), #76 (Karpathy skip list).
What This Means for Every Consulting

This is our pitch, articulated by Karpathy. Our two verticals already map to floor and ceiling:

  • Brooker (non-tech / finance): "Floor-raise" is exactly what she teaches. The Karpathy framing is now the most credible single-quote validation. Add to her opener: "Last year Karpathy named what's happening at the entry level. This year he says he feels behind at the senior level. We teach both — today we're starting at the floor."
  • Mike (tech / Cursor / Claude Code): "Ceiling-raise" is exactly what he teaches. Senior-engineer audiences (Bloomberg, NYT eng) are the highest-leverage buyer because they're the ones Karpathy says are scrambling. Use this in next pitch as the opener.
  • Natalia (sales): the "floor / ceiling" frame is a clean two-slide narrative for any proposal. Slide 1: there are two AI shifts, not one. Slide 2: we're one of the few firms that runs both tracks.
  • Curriculum gap to capture: if no one has yet published "the canonical agentic engineering curriculum," that is a publishable artifact Every Newsletter or Every Consulting could ship. Suggest to Natalia / Dan as a content / lead-gen play.

Headline action: Mike and Natalia agree on a one-page "floor / ceiling" framing slide for the next deck within a week — before this framing becomes everyone's slide.

Anthropic ships Claude Security — vertical app moves up the stack

Claude (@claudeai), amplified by @kimmonismus and @aakashgupta

Anthropic launches Claude Security — Opus 4.7-powered code vulnerability scanner in public beta for Enterprise. First major vertical app shipped by Anthropic itself, going head-to-head with Snyk/Semgrep/SonarQube.

Worth Your Time?

Read. This is the first Anthropic-branded vertical product on top of Claude Code primitives — a meaningful platform→app transition signal, not just another model release.

What's Actually Happening

Anthropic stopped being only a model lab. It's a model lab + a vertical app vendor. The structural reality: - Validation is the moat. Every static analyzer can dump findings; almost none can confidently triage them. LLMs at Opus-4.7 quality can. The product is "fewer false alarms" not "more findings." - Enterprise-only gate. This is a distribution play through existing Claude Enterprise contracts — bypasses the dev-tools sales motion entirely. Snyk/Semgrep have to win each new logo; Anthropic just upsells the seats it already has. - Why now: The Apr-2026 platform tightening (third-party harness restrictions) and this product launch are the same move from two angles — Anthropic capturing more of the value its model creates, instead of letting wrappers monetize on top.

What's Not Being Said
  • Liability. Suggesting patches for security vulns is a different liability surface than suggesting refactors. Anthropic has not (publicly) said how it indemnifies a missed CVE or a bad patch.
  • Regression risk for the ecosystem. If "Claude does X" becomes the default for vertical AI apps, the third-party ecosystem (Cursor, Cline, Continue, the Snyks of the world) compresses fast. Some of those companies are also Anthropic customers.
  • The validation claim is unverified. "Cuts false positives" — by how much, on what benchmark? OX numbers in @aakashgupta's thread aren't Anthropic's claimed performance, they're the size of the problem.
Where This Sits in the Bigger Picture
  • AI trajectory: Foundation labs (Anthropic, OpenAI, Google) are all shipping vertical apps now. OpenAI has Codex/AgentKit/Operator; Google has Jules/Code Assist; Anthropic has had Code/Skills/now Security. The lab-as-platform era is ending; lab-as-app-vendor era starting.
  • Labor market: AppSec engineering roles tilt toward "patch reviewer" rather than "alert triager." Junior AppSec headcount most exposed.
  • Procurement: CISOs at Claude Enterprise shops will get pressured by procurement to consolidate Snyk/Semgrep contracts onto Anthropic. Renewal windows over the next 12 months will see churn.
What's Likely Coming Next
  • Anthropic ships more vertical apps in Q3/Q4: Claude for Legal Discovery, Claude for SOC, Claude for Compliance Audit. The pattern is the same — a place enterprises drown in alerts/documents and where validation > generation.
  • Snyk/Semgrep respond by deepening repo-level context (long-running agents, PR-aware scanning) — areas where a code-host (GitHub) or CI vendor would have data advantage Anthropic doesn't.
  • A nasty incident: Claude Security misses a critical vuln, or auto-suggests a patch that introduces one. Inevitable; the question is timeline.
