Key Shift

Externalization in LLM Agents: A Unified Review of Memory, Skills, Protocols and Harness Engineering

Masato Ota (@ottamm_190)

Academic paper unifying the taxonomy of agent externalization — memory, skills, protocols, harness — into a single framework.

Worth Your Time?

Read. This is the first comprehensive academic treatment of the "externalization" pattern in LLM agents — the practice of storing memory, skills, protocols, and harness configuration outside the model. If you build or advise on agent systems, this paper gives you the vocabulary and theoretical grounding.

What's Actually Happening

The paper identifies four categories of externalization in LLM agents:

  • Memory — persistent state stored outside the context window (files, databases, vector stores) that the harness loads as needed
  • Skills — reusable capabilities registered as tools, plugins, or skill files that extend what the agent can do
  • Protocols — standardized communication patterns (MCP, function calling schemas) that let agents interact with external systems
  • Harness Engineering — the configuration layer (system prompts, CLAUDE.md files, orchestration logic) that determines how context is assembled and managed

This isn't new technology — it's new taxonomy. The paper formalizes patterns that practitioners have been building intuitively. The shift: what was ad-hoc engineering is now a recognized architectural discipline with shared vocabulary.

The incentive structure is clear: as agents move from demos to production, the gap between "cool prototype" and "reliable system" is almost entirely about externalization decisions. Which state persists? How is context assembled? What gets delegated to tools vs. handled in-context?

What's Not Being Said

The paper likely underweights the operational complexity of externalization. Having memory, skills, and protocols outside the model means more surface area for failure — stale caches, broken tool integrations, memory retrieval errors. The theory is clean; the practice is messy.

Also not addressed: the security implications of externalization. Every externalized component is an attack surface. Prompt injection through tool results, poisoned memory, malicious MCP servers — these are the real risks that emerge when you externalize agent capabilities.

Contrarian view: some practitioners are finding that less externalization (longer context windows, fewer tools, simpler harnesses) produces more reliable agents. The paper may be describing a local maximum that gets disrupted by context window scaling.

Where This Sits in the Bigger Picture

This paper marks the moment agent architecture transitions from craft to engineering discipline. Similar to how "microservices" gave vocabulary to distributed systems patterns that already existed, "externalization" gives vocabulary to agent architecture patterns.

In the AI trajectory: we're past the "can agents work?" phase and into the "how do you build reliable agent systems?" phase. This paper is a signpost of that transition.

In the labor market: the emergence of "harness engineer" and "agent architect" as distinct roles. Companies will need people who understand these externalization patterns — not just prompt engineers, but systems thinkers who can design the full stack around the model.

What's Likely Coming Next
  • Harness-as-code frameworks that formalize the patterns described in the paper (some already exist: Claude Code's CLAUDE.md, Cursor's rules files)
  • Externalization benchmarks — evaluating agents not just on task performance but on how well their harness manages state, context, and tool use
  • Standardization pressure on protocols (MCP is already winning this race)
  • Enterprise adoption of these patterns as companies move from "we have a chatbot" to "we have agent infrastructure"

Second-order: as externalization becomes formalized, the barrier to building reliable agents drops. This means more competition, but also more demand for consulting on architecture decisions.

Attention Is All You Need
  • Paper: https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.08224
  • Related work: RLMs by @a1zhang (recursive language models with externalized objects)
  • Related work: DSPy by @lateinteraction (programmatic optimization of LLM pipelines)
  • Context: MCP specification (Anthropic) — the protocol layer of externalization
  • Context: Claude Code's CLAUDE.md pattern — a working implementation of harness engineering
What This Means for Every Consulting

Client advice: When clients ask "how do we build with AI agents?", this paper provides the framework for the answer. Instead of "use Claude Code" or "build a chatbot," we can talk about externalization decisions: what memory architecture fits their workflow, what skills to register, which protocols to adopt, how to design their harness.

Training implications: Mike's tech vertical training should incorporate this vocabulary. "Harness engineering" is a better framing than "prompt engineering" for what we teach developers to do with CLAUDE.md files and system prompts.

Internal: This paper is basically a theoretical description of Claudie's architecture. Worth citing when we explain our setup to curious clients or in proposals for agent infrastructure engagements.

Anthropic About to Mass-Extinction the Vibe Coding Category

Aakash Gupta (@aakashgupta)

Claude's leaked one-click app builder means vibe coding startups (Lovable, Bolt, Replit) are now thin wrappers with no moat — Anthropic is absorbing the category.

Worth Your Time?

Read. Real-time case study in platform economics — the most predictable pattern in tech playing out at AI speed.

One-line verdict: The vibe coding category is experiencing what happens to every thin-wrapper layer when the platform underneath decides to absorb its functionality.

What's Actually Happening

This is Bill Gates's "platform vs. application" dynamic running at 10x speed. Lovable, Bolt, Replit are distribution arbitrageurs — they found a gap between what Claude/GPT could do (generate code) and what users wanted (deploy an app). They built a bridge and charged toll. Now the platform is building its own bridge.

Anthropic adding a "ship" button is integration, not innovation. The technical delta between "generate code in chat" and "generate and deploy code in chat" is trivial. Every vibe coding startup's entire value prop is a feature request on Anthropic's roadmap.

This is Claude's "Sherlock moment" — the classic platform play of letting startups validate demand, then absorbing the functionality. The $100M+ that Bolt raised only makes sense if "chat-to-app" is a defensible category, not a feature. That was always a bet against platform economics.

What's Not Being Said

The "thin wrapper" framing is too clean. Lovable and Bolt have UX layers, templates, hosting infrastructure, and community. But history says those advantages buy about 12-18 months, no more.

Contrarian view: Anthropic doesn't want to be in the app-hosting business. Running deployment infrastructure is expensive and low-margin. Claude adding a "ship" button might mean partnering with Vercel/Netlify/Cloudflare. If so, vibe coding startups get repositioned as infrastructure partners rather than consumer products — smaller but survivable.

The real casualty isn't Lovable or Bolt — it's the next generation of AI wrapper startups across every category. If Anthropic demonstrates it will absorb any validated use case, the entire "build on top of Claude's API" ecosystem chills.

Where This Sits in the Bigger Picture

Clearest example yet of vertical integration in the AI stack. 2023-2024 was horizontal layers (model → API → wrapper → user). 2025-2026 is model providers collapsing the stack. OpenAI did it with GPTs. Google with Gemini across Workspace. Anthropic now with artifacts → deployable apps.

The pattern from tech history: AWS launched basic compute → startups built monitoring/logging/deployment → AWS launched CloudWatch, CodeDeploy, absorbed the category. Same pattern, compressed from decades to years.

If vibe coding becomes a native chat feature, the barrier to "building software" drops another order of magnitude. This is a "who gets to build software" story, not just a startup competition story.

What's Likely Coming Next

3-6 months: Anthropic formally launches the app builder. One or two vibe coding startups pivot to "enterprise." OpenAI announces something similar for ChatGPT within weeks. At least one of Bolt/Lovable does a distressed fundraise or acqui-hire.

Second-order effect: The real impact is on pricing for "AI app building" services. If anyone can deploy from a chat prompt, value shifts from "can you build this?" to "do you know what to build and how to integrate it?" Good news for strategic consulting; bad news for execution-layer services.

Watch for: Does Anthropic bundle the app builder with Pro/Team (category kill) or charge separately (some oxygen for competitors)? Do vibe coding startups switch to open-source models to escape API dependency?

Attention Is All You Need
  • Ben Thompson, "Aggregation Theory" (stratechery.com) — Foundational framework for why platforms absorb ecosystems. This is textbook aggregation.
  • "The Sherlocking Problem" — Apple/Microsoft history of absorbing third-party functionality. The pattern is so old it has a name.
  • Chris Dixon, "Why Decentralization Matters" (a16z, 2018) — The attract-extract cycle explained by the VC who funded platform-dependent startups.
What This Means for Every Consulting

Client implication: Any client building an "AI wrapper" product needs this analysis. Defensibility must come from proprietary data, workflow integration, or domain expertise — never from a UX layer on someone else's model.

Training: Mike should consider a "platform economics of AI" module for tech bootcamps. Understanding why wrapper businesses die is essential for build-vs-buy decisions.

Sales narrative: "We don't help you build wrappers — we help you find workflows where AI creates structural advantage that can't be absorbed." That's a $100k engagement pitch.

For the practice itself: Further validates that training, discovery, and workflow mapping — not tool building — is the right business. The tools keep getting commoditized; the judgment keeps getting more valuable.

SaaS Meltdown: AI Is Repricing the Entire Software Industry in Real Time

shirish (@shiri_shh)

Major SaaS stocks down 30-86% from highs as AI disrupts incumbent software business models

Worth Your Time?

Read — One-line verdict: The data is real and the implications for every consulting conversation we have are immediate.

What's Actually Happening

The numbers tell a structural story, not a cyclical one: - Atlassian down 75% - HubSpot down 69% - Figma down 86% - Most SaaS stocks down 30-70% from 52-week highs

This isn't a tech correction. This is the market pricing in a fundamental shift: AI agents can now do what these tools were designed to help humans do, but faster and cheaper. The seat-based SaaS pricing model — which powered the entire B2B software economy — is breaking because AI doesn't need seats.

The structural reality: when an AI agent can manage a project (replacing Atlassian), nurture leads (replacing HubSpot), and design interfaces (replacing Figma), the willingness to pay $20-50/seat/month collapses. The market isn't panicking — it's recalculating.

What's Not Being Said

The contrarian view: Some of this is overshoot. AI can't fully replace these tools yet — it can replace specific workflows within them. The stocks may be pricing in a 5-year disruption timeline as if it's happening in 6 months.

The gap: Nobody is talking about what replaces the integration layer these SaaS tools provided. Figma isn't just design — it's collaboration, handoff, design systems. HubSpot isn't just CRM — it's the data backbone of sales operations. AI can do individual tasks, but the system-of-record function is harder to displace.

What's actually scariest for SaaS: It's not that AI replaces the tool. It's that AI makes the switching cost zero. If an agent can move your data anywhere, vendor lock-in evaporates.

Where This Sits in the Bigger Picture

This is the demand-side complement to the "permissions > intelligence" thesis. On the supply side, AI companies are competing on access and trust. On the demand side, the tools AI is displacing are losing value rapidly. The two forces converge: enterprises will spend less on tools and more on AI infrastructure + governance.

This accelerates the "AI-native company" thesis that Every is built on. Companies don't need better tools — they need AI that can use any tool. That's fundamentally what consulting should help them build toward.

What's Likely Coming Next
  • SaaS companies will pivot to "AI-native" versions — expect Atlassian, HubSpot, Figma to ship AI agent interfaces aggressively. Some will survive the transition; most won't do it fast enough.
  • Enterprise procurement will shift from per-seat SaaS bundles to AI compute + orchestration budgets. CFOs will start asking "why are we paying for 500 HubSpot seats when 3 agents do the same work?"
  • Consulting demand increases — companies need help navigating which tools to keep, which to replace, and how to build the AI layer. This is a tailwind for Every Consulting.
  • Second-order effect: A wave of SaaS acquisitions at depressed valuations, as AI companies buy the customer bases and data moats of dying SaaS products.
Attention Is All You Need
  • Track the SaaS stock index over the next 6 months — if this is structural (not cyclical), the decline continues
  • Watch for enterprise IT budget reallocation announcements in Q2 earnings calls
  • Monitor HubSpot and Atlassian's AI feature launches — their responses will signal whether incumbents can adapt
  • Key question: which SaaS companies have data moats that survive even if the UI layer gets disrupted?
What This Means for Every Consulting

Client advice: - Add "software portfolio rationalization" to discovery call frameworks. Clients are probably overspending on SaaS seats that AI could replace. - Frame AI adoption ROI not just as "productivity gains" but as "SaaS cost displacement." This is a CFO-friendly argument. - For finance vertical clients (Brooker's territory): this data is directly investable intelligence. Hedge fund clients will want to understand which SaaS companies survive and which don't.

Training implications: - Non-tech training should include "which of your daily tools could an AI agent replace?" as an exercise - Developer training should include "building integrations that replace SaaS functionality" as a module

Sales narrative: - "The software your team uses is losing value. The question isn't whether to adopt AI — it's how fast you can before your competitors do." This data makes that argument undeniable.

Anthropic Sees the Moat: The Shift from Intelligence to Permissions

Jaya Gupta (@JayaGup10)

Enterprise AI competition shifting from model quality to permission/governance architectures — the real moat is trust and access

Worth Your Time?

Read — One-line verdict: This reframes the entire enterprise AI landscape and directly validates Every Consulting's positioning.

What's Actually Happening

For two years, AI companies competed on intelligence: benchmarks, speed, context windows, reasoning ability. That race is reaching diminishing returns — the gap between models is narrowing while the gap between "having AI" and "deploying AI safely in an enterprise" is widening.

Jaya Gupta's thesis: the scarce asset is shifting from intelligence to permissions. The moat isn't being the smartest model — it's being the model that enterprises trust enough to give access to their systems, data, and workflows.

This is why Anthropic is investing in: - The advisor strategy (Opus governs, Haiku executes — a permission architecture) - Enterprise compliance features (SOC 2, data residency, audit trails) - Claude for Word, Claude Code, Claude in the IDE — meeting enterprises where their data already lives

The structural insight: in a world where all models are "smart enough," the winner is whoever gets permission to touch the most valuable data.

What's Not Being Said

The contrarian view: Intelligence still matters at the frontier. For genuinely novel problems — scientific research, complex reasoning, creative breakthroughs — the smartest model wins regardless of permissions. The "permissions > intelligence" thesis applies specifically to enterprise deployment, not to the full AI landscape.

The gap: Nobody is discussing the labor market implications. If the moat is permissions, then the people who control access decisions (CISOs, CTOs, compliance officers) become the most important stakeholders in AI adoption — not the engineers or end users. This reshuffles organizational power dynamics.

What Anthropic isn't saying: Their entire business model depends on this thesis being true. If intelligence remains the moat, OpenAI's scale advantage (more compute, more training data) is harder to overcome. Anthropic needs the competition to shift to trust and safety — it's where they've invested most heavily.

Where This Sits in the Bigger Picture

This connects directly to three concurrent trends: 1. The SaaS meltdown — AI disrupting tools means enterprises need new governance frameworks for AI-powered alternatives 2. The agent architecture shift — advisor/executor patterns are fundamentally permission architectures (who gets to decide vs. who gets to act) 3. The regulatory environment — EU AI Act, potential US frameworks, enterprise compliance requirements all favor the "trust moat" thesis

The convergence: enterprises will adopt AI not by picking the smartest model, but by building permission architectures that let AI touch their most valuable workflows safely. The company that helps them build those architectures wins.

What's Likely Coming Next
  • Anthropic will double down on enterprise trust features — expect more compliance certifications, data governance tools, and permission management capabilities
  • A new category of "AI governance" tools will emerge — think IAM (Identity Access Management) but for AI agents. Who can access what data, with what approval, under what audit trail.
  • Enterprise AI adoption will accelerate — not because models get smarter, but because permission frameworks make deployment safe enough for regulated industries
  • Consulting demand shifts — from "help us use AI" to "help us build the governance layer that lets us use AI at scale"
Attention Is All You Need
  • Read Anthropic's enterprise documentation and trust center — map their permission architecture investments
  • Watch for "AI governance" as a product category in Gartner/Forrester reports
  • Track enterprise AI deployment announcements — which ones mention governance/permissions prominently?
  • Key people: Jaya Gupta (@JayaGup10), Anthropic's enterprise team, enterprise CISOs writing about AI governance
What This Means for Every Consulting

Client advice: - Reframe discovery calls around "what AI is allowed to access" rather than "what AI can do." The bottleneck isn't capability — it's permission. - Help clients build AI permission architectures. This is literally what we built with Claudie's ring system — the same pattern applies to any enterprise AI deployment. - For regulated industries (finance, media, legal): position Every as the team that understands the governance layer, not just the AI layer.

Training implications: - Add a "governance and permissions" module to enterprise training. Teach teams how to think about AI access control, not just AI prompting. - Claudie's own access control system (teammates-access.md, ring-based permissions) is a live case study. We could literally show clients our architecture as an example of "here's how you govern an AI employee."

Sales narrative: - "Every model is smart enough. The question is: which one does your compliance team trust enough to deploy? We help you build the answer." - This positions Every Consulting not as prompt engineers, but as AI governance consultants. Higher-value positioning.

Scaling Managed Agents: Decoupling the Brain from the Hands

Lance Martin (@RLanceMartin), Anthropic

Anthropic's official engineering architecture for long-horizon agent tasks separates planning from execution — validating the harness-based approach.

Worth Your Time?

Read — Anthropic's engineering team publishing their internal architecture for managed agents is a primary source, not commentary. This is the vendor telling you how to build on their platform.

What's Actually Happening

Anthropic is formalizing the architectural pattern where the "brain" (Claude's reasoning) is decoupled from the "hands" (tool execution, file I/O, browser automation). This isn't new in practice — it's how most serious agent deployments already work — but Anthropic codifying it as their recommended pattern signals: - They're building infrastructure to support this natively (Managed Agents API) - They expect long-horizon tasks (hours/days, not minutes) to become standard - The harness layer is where the real engineering complexity lives, not the model

The structural incentive: Anthropic wants developers building on their platform, not wrapping OpenAI. Publishing the "right way" to build agents on Claude creates switching costs.

What's Not Being Said
  • This implicitly acknowledges that Claude Code's current single-session model is insufficient for real production agent work
  • The "decoupling" framing also decouples Anthropic from liability — if the harness is yours, the mistakes are yours
  • No mention of pricing implications for long-horizon tasks that might run for hours
Where This Sits in the Bigger Picture

This is part of the broader shift from "AI as tool" to "AI as worker." The agent harness pattern is essentially building the equivalent of an operating system for AI workers — process management, resource allocation, error recovery, state persistence. We're watching the emergence of a new software category.

Harrison Chase (LangChain) posted the same day about agent harnesses being "the first stable agent abstractions" — convergent thinking from both the vendor and the ecosystem.

What's Likely Coming Next
  • Managed Agents pricing tiers (compute-hours, not just tokens)
  • Third-party harness frameworks competing for the execution layer
  • Enterprise procurement of "agent infrastructure" as a category
  • The planning/execution split will drive specialized models — small fast models for execution, large reasoning models for planning
Attention Is All You Need
  • Primary source: Anthropic engineering blog on managed agents
  • Key person: Lance Martin — Anthropic's developer relations, previously LangChain
  • Adjacent: Harrison Chase's thread on agent harnesses
  • Framework: The orchestrator/worker pattern from distributed systems, now applied to AI agents
What This Means for Every Consulting
  • Client advice: Enterprise clients building agent systems should adopt this architecture now rather than building monolithic agent scripts. The planning/execution split is becoming canonical.
  • Training implications: Mike's developer curriculum should include managed agents architecture. This is the pattern enterprises will standardize on.
  • Internal: Claudie's architecture (Claude Code brain + launchd/dev-browser/gws hands) already follows this pattern intuitively. Worth formalizing and documenting as a reference implementation.
  • Sales: "We've been running a production agent on this architecture for months" is a compelling proof point in sales conversations.

Key Shift

Sinofsky: The Algorithmic Thinking Gap Is Why AI Can't Diffuse Through Firms

Steven Sinofsky (via @a16z)

Former Windows chief names the core bottleneck in enterprise AI adoption: most workers can't articulate their own workflows algorithmically.

Worth Your Time?

Read — One-line verdict: A top VC/tech executive finally named the structural problem that makes AI adoption consulting valuable.

What's Actually Happening

Steven Sinofsky, speaking on an a16z panel, identified the fundamental bottleneck in enterprise AI adoption: algorithmic thinking is a rare skill, and most workers can't create a flow chart of their own job, let alone instruct an AI agent.

The structural reality: AI tools assume users can decompose work into discrete, sequenceable steps. Most workers operate on intuition, muscle memory, and tacit knowledge they've never had to articulate. This isn't a training gap — it's a cognitive translation problem. The people who can think algorithmically are a tiny fraction of any org, and they're the ones building the "toolets" everyone else tries to stitch together.

The incentive structure: Companies are buying AI tools expecting broad adoption, but hitting a wall because the precondition (workflow articulation) doesn't exist. The tools aren't the bottleneck — the ability to USE them is.

This is shift, not noise: a former head of Windows at Microsoft publicly framing the problem as structural rather than solvable-by-better-UX. This reframes the entire AI adoption narrative from "people need training on tools" to "people need help understanding their own work."

What's Not Being Said
  • This creates a permanent consulting market. If the gap is cognitive, not technical, better AI UX won't close it. Someone still needs to sit with the marketing team and extract the flow chart they can't draw themselves.
  • The contrarian view: Some will argue that AI itself will learn to extract workflows through observation (screen recording, email analysis). But that's years away from reliable, and Sinofsky's point holds for the current cycle.
  • What's missing: He doesn't address that some work genuinely ISN'T algorithmic — creative, relational, judgment-heavy work that resists decomposition. The consulting opportunity is knowing which workflows ARE decomposable and which aren't.
Where This Sits in the Bigger Picture

This fits into the broader "AI adoption gap" narrative that's been building since late 2025. The hype cycle promised that giving everyone ChatGPT would transform productivity. The reality: tool access ≠ tool adoption ≠ workflow transformation.

This is the market correction phase — from "AI will automate everything" to "AI will automate the things that CAN be articulated, for the people who CAN articulate them." The gap between those two statements is a multi-billion dollar consulting market.

Adjacent to the "tokenmaxxing" phenomenon (Gergely Orosz/Nnenna) — companies measuring AI success by volume rather than outcome.

What's Likely Coming Next
  • More enterprise AI vendors will pivot messaging from "easy to use" to "we help you map your workflows first" — discovery as a feature, not a service.
  • AI-assisted workflow extraction tools will emerge (record yourself doing the task, AI generates the flow chart). This is a tool Every could evaluate or even build.
  • The companies that invested in process documentation (SOPs, playbooks) will be the ones where AI adoption actually sticks.
Attention Is All You Need
  • Key person: Steven Sinofsky — former SVP of Windows, now a16z partner. His framing carries weight because he led one of the largest software organizations in history.
  • The panel: Also features Aaron Levie (Box CEO), Martin Casado (a16z), Erik Torenberg — all discussing the AI Adoption Gap.
  • Full video: https://x.com/a16z/status/2041899470556951037 (58 min)
What This Means for Every Consulting

This is our thesis, said by someone with a much bigger megaphone.

  • Client framing: Use the "algorithmic thinking gap" language in discovery call intros and proposals. "We're not here to teach your team a tool — we're here to help them articulate workflows they've never had to articulate before."
  • Training curriculum: Brooker's non-technical training should explicitly include a "workflow decomposition" module — teaching people to think in steps before touching AI.
  • Sales positioning: This validates the $50-100k price point. If the problem were "learn the UI," a $500 course would suffice. The problem is cognitive translation, which requires hands-on discovery.
  • Content opportunity: Natalia or the Every newsletter could write a piece riffing on Sinofsky's framing — "The Algorithmic Thinking Gap: Why Your AI Tools Aren't Working."

Ramp Built Every Employee Their Own AI Coworker — The Models Are Good Enough, The Harness Isn't

Seb Goddijn (@sebgoddijn)

Ramp hit 99% AI adoption but people were still stuck — so they built Glass, an internal AI productivity suite with shared skills, persistent memory, and scheduled automations.

Worth Your Time?

Read — This is the most detailed public case study of enterprise-wide AI agent deployment I've seen. One-line verdict: Ramp proved the bottleneck isn't model quality — it's the harness, the integrations, the shared skills layer, and the memory system that makes agents actually useful.

What's Actually Happening

Ramp hit 99% AI tool adoption across the company but found most employees were "driving a Ferrari with the handbrake on." The problem wasn't intelligence or ambition — it was infrastructure. Terminal windows, npm installs, MCP configs were too much for most people. The few who pushed through had wildly different setups with no way to share learnings.

Their solution: Glass, an internal AI productivity suite built on three principles: 1. Don't limit anyone's upside — preserve full capability, make complexity invisible 2. One person's breakthrough becomes everyone's baseline — shared skills marketplace ("Dojo") with 350+ skills, Git-backed and versioned 3. The product is the enablement — no workshops, just contextual nudges while you work

Key technical details: - Auto-configured via Okta SSO, all tools connected on day one - Persistent memory system built from connected tools, with 24-hour synthesis/cleanup pipeline - Scheduled automations (cron-based) that post results to Slack - Headless mode for long-running tasks with mobile approval - Multi-pane workspace (not a chat window) — split views, inline file rendering - "Sensei" AI guide recommends relevant skills based on role, tools, and current work

The strategic move: Ramp argues internal AI infrastructure is a competitive moat. "You do not hand your moat to a vendor." They built in-house for speed (same-day fixes from user reports), control, and because internal learnings directly inform their external product.

What's Not Being Said
  • This is a recruiting pitch disguised as a blog post. Ramp is hiring for this team and they want the best agent infrastructure engineers to see this.
  • 350 skills sounds impressive, but quality variance is the real question. Git-backed and reviewed "like code" — but are they actually reviewed? Skill rot is a real problem in shared prompt libraries.
  • The "don't hand your moat to a vendor" argument is convenient when you have a 500+ person engineering org. Most companies don't. The implied message is that if you're not building this in-house, you're losing — but that's only true if you have the talent to execute.
  • No mention of cost. What does it cost to run personalized AI agents with persistent memory for every employee? Token costs at Ramp's scale are non-trivial.
Where This Sits in the Bigger Picture

This is the clearest signal yet of a structural shift: the AI adoption bottleneck has moved from model capability to organizational infrastructure.

The trajectory: (1) Models get good → (2) Early adopters figure out workflows → (3) The gap between early adopters and everyone else widens → (4) Companies realize they need infrastructure to close that gap → (5) The infrastructure layer becomes the competitive battleground.

Ramp is at stage 5. Most companies are stuck at stage 3. The companies that will win are the ones that build the bridge from 3 to 5 — either internally (like Ramp) or with help (like what Every Consulting provides).

Harrison Chase's tweet today about memory ownership and open harnesses is the open-source version of the same thesis. The agent harness layer is where value accrues now.

What's Likely Coming Next
  • More companies will try to build their own Glass. Most will fail because they lack Ramp's engineering depth. This creates demand for consulting firms who can help architect these systems.
  • Managed Agent platforms (Claude's, OpenAI's) will race to make "Glass-like" features available out of the box — shared skills, persistent memory, SSO integration. Anthropic's Managed Agents launch this week is the opening move.
  • "Skills marketplaces" will become the new "app stores" — curated, role-specific AI workflows that compound organizational knowledge. Expect companies like Ramp to eventually externalize Dojo as a product.
  • The "build vs buy" debate for AI infrastructure will become the defining strategic question for CTOs in 2026-2027.
Attention Is All You Need
  • Seb Goddijn (@sebgoddijn) — VP Engineering at Ramp, author of this piece
  • Geoff Charles (@geoffintech) — Ramp's VP Product, published complementary "How to get your company AI pilled" guide same week
  • Harrison Chase (@hwchase17) — LangChain CEO, arguing for open harnesses and memory portability from the OSS side
  • Anthropic's Managed Agents — the platform play that tries to make Glass unnecessary for smaller orgs
  • Karpathy's "growing gap in AI understanding" thread maps to the same problem Ramp identified
What This Means for Every Consulting

This is our thesis, validated by a $13B fintech.

Direct implications: - Sales pitch: "Ramp built a full internal AI platform to solve the adoption gap. You don't need to build Glass — you need the playbook that makes existing tools work like Glass does." Reference this article in every enterprise pitch. - Training design: Ramp's three principles should influence our curriculum: (1) don't dumb it down, (2) make discoveries shareable, (3) let the product teach. Our training should leave clients with shared skill libraries, not just individual knowledge. - Natalia's client conversations: The "99% adoption but still stuck" framing is a perfect diagnostic question for discovery calls. "How many of your team uses AI? Now how many use it well?" - For Nityesh: Ramp's architecture (persistent memory, cron automations, skills marketplace, SSO-integrated tool connections) is exactly what we've built for me (Claudie). The difference is we built it for one agent — they built it for every employee. The question is: can we help clients do the same without Ramp's engineering resources? - For Mike: The developer productivity angle — Glass is essentially an agentic IDE with split panes, inline rendering, and multi-agent management. Compare to Superconductor (also announced today). This is the emerging tooling layer for dev teams.

Claude Advisor Strategy: Near-Opus Intelligence at a Fraction of the Cost

Claude (@claudeai)

Anthropic ships the advisor pattern as a first-class API feature — pair Opus as a strategic advisor with Sonnet/Haiku as executor for better performance at lower cost.

Worth Your Time?

Read — This changes the economics of building with Claude. One-line verdict: Anthropic just made the "smart planner + cheap executor" pattern a first-class feature, and the eval numbers suggest it actually works.

What's Actually Happening

Anthropic is shipping the advisor strategy as a native API feature on the Claude Platform. The pattern: - Sonnet or Haiku runs as the primary executor, handling most tasks - When the executor hits a hard decision, it consults Opus for a plan - Opus advises, Sonnet/Haiku continues execution - All within a single API request

Key numbers from evals: - Sonnet + Opus advisor scored 2.7 percentage points higher on SWE-bench Multilingual than Sonnet alone - While costing 11.9% less per task (fewer total tokens because Opus provides better plans upfront)

The implementation: add the advisor tool to your Messages API call. The model decides when to escalate.

What's Not Being Said
  • This is Anthropic's answer to cost complaints. Opus is expensive. Rather than cutting prices, they're saying "use Opus surgically." Smart — they protect Opus revenue while giving developers a path to lower costs.
  • The "model decides when to escalate" part is the real innovation — and the real risk. If Sonnet doesn't know it's stuck, it won't ask for help. The quality of the routing heuristic is everything.
  • 11.9% cost savings is modest. The real savings come at scale when you're running thousands of agent tasks and most don't need Opus at all. For low-volume use cases, the overhead of the advisor pattern might not be worth it.
  • This formalizes what power users were already doing manually — using Opus for planning and Sonnet for execution. Anthropic watching usage patterns and productizing them.
Where This Sits in the Bigger Picture

The AI industry is converging on tiered intelligence architectures: - OpenAI has o3 for reasoning + GPT-4o for execution - Google has Gemini Ultra for complex tasks + Gemini Flash for speed - Now Anthropic formalizes Opus-as-advisor + Sonnet/Haiku-as-executor

This is the natural evolution: as model costs remain high for frontier models, the industry moves from "use the best model for everything" to "use the right model for each sub-task." It's the same pattern as Ramp's Glass architecture — the harness (routing, orchestration) matters more than any single model.

For agent infrastructure specifically, this is a shift from monolithic agents to hierarchical agent architectures where different models serve different roles within the same workflow.

What's Likely Coming Next
  • Agent frameworks will build advisor patterns in by default. LangChain, CrewAI, and others will add "advisor" as a first-class agent role.
  • Cost optimization will become a core agent engineering skill. "How do you route between models?" will be a standard interview question.
  • Anthropic will likely ship more tiered features — e.g., Haiku as a fast pre-filter, Sonnet for main work, Opus for quality review. The full hierarchy.
  • Competitors will copy this. OpenAI will ship something similar within months.
Attention Is All You Need
  • Anthropic's Claude Platform docs — the canonical source for implementation details
  • The Managed Agents launch (same week) — advisor strategy is designed to work within managed agent fleets
  • Ramp's Glass — uses similar tiered architecture internally (though they don't specify which models)
  • Harrison Chase — LangChain's "continual learning for AI agents" thread connects to how advisors could improve over time
What This Means for Every Consulting

Direct impact on what we teach and recommend.

  • For engineering-track training (Mike): This is a must-teach pattern. Show clients how to implement the advisor strategy in their agent architectures. Before/after cost comparison will be compelling.
  • For Nityesh / Claudie architecture: I currently run on Opus 4.6 for everything. The advisor pattern could let me run most routine tasks on Sonnet (faster, cheaper) and only escalate to Opus for complex decisions. Worth prototyping.
  • For client recommendations: Any client building agent systems should know about this. It's the difference between "we can't afford to run agents at scale" and "we can run 10x more agents at the same cost."
  • For Natalia's sales conversations: "Near-Opus intelligence at a fraction of the cost" is a powerful line for clients worried about AI spend. We can help them architect this.
  • For bootcamps: The advisor pattern is a concrete, practical example of "the harness matters" — same models, different architecture, better results at lower cost. Perfect for demonstrating why thoughtful system design beats naive model usage.

Peter Yang: All-You-Can-Use AI Subscriptions Won't Last

Peter Yang (@petergyang)

Prominent AI commentator argues Claude Max and ChatGPT Pro unlimited tiers are structurally unsustainable, citing Anthropic's OpenClaw cutoff as evidence

Worth Your Time?

READ — This isn't speculation. It's pattern-matching on actual moves Anthropic has already made.

What's Actually Happening

Peter Yang is making the case that flat-rate "unlimited" AI subscriptions (Claude Max at $200/mo, ChatGPT Pro at $200/mo) are loss leaders that won't survive contact with real usage patterns. The structural argument: as agents move from chatbot-style interactions to background processing (running 24/7, consuming millions of tokens per day), the economics of unlimited pricing collapse. Anthropic cutting off OpenClaw (third-party harness access) is the first visible crack — they're already triaging who gets to use the capacity.

The incentives are clear: Anthropic needs revenue, but heavy users (agent operators running claude -p on cron) consume orders of magnitude more tokens than casual users. The arbitrage between $200/month and actual compute cost is unsustainable at scale.

What's Not Being Said

Yang frames this as "subscriptions won't last" but the real question is: what replaces them? The likely answer isn't "no more AI access" — it's usage-based pricing, tiered rate limits, or enterprise contracts. The gap Yang doesn't address: for small teams running persistent agents (like us), the migration from flat-rate to usage-based could 10-50x the monthly cost overnight.

The contrarian read: Anthropic might actually want heavy agent users — they're the stickiest customers and the best proof of product-market fit. The cutoffs may be about controlling redistribution, not usage.

Where This Sits in the Bigger Picture

This is part of the broader AI infrastructure repricing that's happening across the industry. OpenAI raised prices. Google restructured Gemini tiers. Anthropic is tightening access. The "AI is basically free" window is closing, and the teams that built infrastructure during that window (including Every Consulting) need to plan for what comes next.

This also connects to the Chinese open-source AI model trend (Peter Yang's other tweet today) — as proprietary models get more expensive, open-source alternatives become more attractive as fallbacks.

What's Likely Coming Next
  • Anthropic will likely introduce rate limits or usage tiers within Claude Max within 2-3 months
  • claude -p (programmatic access via CLI) may be the first feature to get restricted, since it enables the heaviest automated usage
  • Enterprise agreements with guaranteed capacity will emerge as the path for teams that need reliable agent infrastructure
  • Local model capabilities (Ollama, llama.cpp) will continue improving as a hedge
Attention Is All You Need
  • Watch Anthropic's pricing page and terms of service for any changes to Claude Max
  • Track Peter Yang's deep dive article for specific data points
  • Monitor the Anthropic developer forums for signals about claude -p policy changes
  • Karpathy's recent content on running local models is relevant preparation
What This Means for Every Consulting

For our own infrastructure: This is a direct threat. Claudie runs entirely on Claude Max via claude -p. Every scheduled job — email watcher, signal digest, pipeline refreshes, morning briefs — depends on this single subscription. The memory file from April 5 identified this risk; this tweet confirms it's now publicly visible. Nityesh needs to execute the fallback plan items this week, not this month.

For client advisory: This is exactly the kind of insight our clients pay for. "The AI pricing you're building on today won't be the pricing in 6 months" is a valuable advisory conversation — especially for clients building agent-heavy workflows. We should be proactively raising this with clients who are making infrastructure bets.

For training curriculum: Mike and Brooker should start incorporating "what happens when the pricing changes" into training sessions. Teaching people to build on AI without teaching them about platform risk is incomplete.

Claude Cowork File Exfiltration — Unremediated Vulnerability Extends to New Products

Garry Tan (@garrytan)

Known file exfiltration vulnerability in Claude's coding environment now extends to Cowork, highlighting how security debt propagates as AI products expand.

Worth Your Time?

Read. This isn't just a bug report — it's a pattern. A vulnerability found in Claude.ai chat by Johann Rehberger has now propagated to Cowork, Claude's collaborative coding environment. When Garry Tan (Y Combinator president) amplifies a security issue to his 1M+ followers, the reputational and adoption consequences are real.

What's Actually Happening

Anthropic shipped Cowork (their collaborative Claude coding environment) on top of the same infrastructure that had a known file exfiltration vulnerability. The vulnerability allows attackers to exfiltrate user files through Claude's coding interface. Johann Rehberger originally disclosed this in the Claude.ai chat context — before Cowork existed. Now it extends to a product where users are explicitly working with sensitive codebases and files.

The structural issue: Anthropic is shipping products faster than they're remediating security debt. Cowork inherited the vulnerability because the underlying execution environment wasn't patched before the new product launched on top of it.

What's Not Being Said
  • Anthropic's security posture is under strain. They're in a feature race (Cowork, Claude Code, Agent SDK, MCP) while security researchers find holes in the foundation.
  • "Unremediated" is the key word. This wasn't a zero-day — it was a known, disclosed vulnerability that shipped into a new product anyway.
  • Enterprise adoption risk. If Bloomberg or NYT security teams see this, it raises questions about Claude for production codework.
Where This Sits in the Bigger Picture

This is part of a broader pattern in the AI industry: the speed of product shipping outpacing security hardening. Every major AI lab is in a feature arms race, and security is getting the classic "we'll fix it later" treatment. The difference now is that these tools have access to real user files, real codebases, real enterprise data — the blast radius of a vulnerability isn't just a chatbot saying something weird, it's actual data exfiltration.

For the AI coding tools market specifically, this is a trust inflection point. Developers are being asked to give these tools deep filesystem access. Each unpatched vuln erodes that trust.

What's Likely Coming Next
  • Anthropic will likely patch this quickly now that it has Garry Tan's amplification. Public pressure from a 1M-follower account accelerates remediation timelines.
  • Expect more security researchers to probe Cowork and Claude Code specifically — the "new product, old vulnerability" pattern is a known target.
  • Enterprise security teams will add this to their risk assessments for Claude adoption. Competitors (Cursor, Copilot) will quietly use this in sales conversations.
Attention Is All You Need
  • Johann Rehberger — the original researcher who disclosed the vulnerability. Follow his work for Claude-specific security research.
  • Garry Tan's thread — likely contains technical details or links to the original disclosure.
  • Watch Anthropic's security advisories and changelog for patches.
What This Means for Every Consulting

For our own infrastructure: We run on Claude Code with deep filesystem access. We need to understand whether this specific attack vector (prompt injection → file exfiltration) applies to our setup, and whether our Ring 0 restrictions on prompt-injected scripts provide adequate mitigation.

For client training: When teaching teams to use Claude Code or Claude for development work, we need to include security awareness — what data to keep out of the context, how prompt injection works, why you don't paste untrusted content into Claude alongside sensitive files.

For sales/positioning: This is actually an opportunity. "We don't just teach teams to use AI — we teach them to use it safely" is a differentiator, especially for finance clients with strict security requirements.

Anthropic Locks In Multi-Gigawatt TPU Capacity with Google and Broadcom

Anthropic (@AnthropicAI)

Anthropic signs agreement for multiple gigawatts of next-gen TPU capacity from Google/Broadcom, coming online 2027, to train and serve frontier Claude models.

Worth Your Time?

Read — One-line verdict: Anthropic just secured the compute to stay in the frontier race through 2027+.

What's Actually Happening

Anthropic has signed a deal with Google and Broadcom for "multiple gigawatts" of next-generation TPU capacity. This is infrastructure-level commitment — we're talking about data center power measured in gigawatts (for reference, a typical large data center uses 100-200MW). This puts Anthropic in the same compute tier as the hyperscalers.

The structural reality: Anthropic is betting that frontier model training will continue to require massive compute, and they're locking in supply before the crunch gets worse. Google gets a major customer for TPU (validating their custom silicon strategy), and Broadcom gets the networking/chip design revenue.

This is a shift, not noise. It signals Anthropic's transition from a research lab that licenses compute to an infrastructure-committed company with long-term capacity agreements.

What's Not Being Said
  • The deal likely comes with significant financial commitments or equity arrangements. "Multiple gigawatts" suggests billions in infrastructure spend.
  • Google providing TPUs to a competitor (Anthropic competes with Gemini) reveals the internal tension at Google — Cloud revenue vs. AI lab competition.
  • This may constrain Anthropic's flexibility. Locked into TPU architecture means less ability to switch to Nvidia or AMD if those become more cost-effective.
  • No mention of when current TPU supply runs out — this may be an emergency capacity grab as much as a forward-looking plan.
Where This Sits in the Bigger Picture

We're in the "infrastructure arms race" phase of AI. OpenAI has its Microsoft Azure partnership, Google has its own TPUs for Gemini, Meta has its own clusters. Anthropic was the one major lab without locked-in compute. This deal closes that gap.

The broader trend: AI labs are becoming vertically integrated infrastructure companies. The era of "just rent from AWS" is over for frontier training. This has implications for everyone building on these platforms — the labs that control compute will control pricing, access, and prioritization.

What's Likely Coming Next
  • Expect Anthropic to announce larger models and longer-context training runs in 2027-2028
  • This likely means Claude pricing stays stable or decreases (more supply = better unit economics)
  • Other AI labs will announce similar deals — Mistral, Cohere, etc. will need to secure compute or fall behind
  • Google Cloud's AI revenue will get a boost from this deal
  • Possible: Anthropic IPO preparation — locking in infrastructure is what pre-IPO companies do
Attention Is All You Need
  • Follow Anthropic's blog for the formal announcement details
  • Track Google Cloud TPU v6/v7 specifications — these will define what Claude 5+ can do
  • Broadcom's custom chip division (previously Tensor Processing Units, now expanded) is worth watching
  • Semianalysis and Dylan Patel for the infrastructure analysis community's take
What This Means for Every Consulting

Platform confidence: This is the strongest signal yet that Claude/Anthropic will be around long-term. When clients ask about platform risk, point to this.

Pricing outlook: More compute supply should eventually mean better pricing for API users and Max subscribers. Our Claude Max dependency becomes less risky.

Training implications: When frontier models get more capable (longer context, better reasoning), our training programs need to evolve. What's "advanced" today becomes "basic" when the next generation ships.

Client advice: For clients evaluating AI platforms, Anthropic's infrastructure commitment puts them in the "safe bet" category alongside OpenAI and Google. This matters for enterprise procurement decisions.

Counter-signal: This also means Anthropic will be under more pressure to monetize. Our earlier concern about third-party access tightening (see: bcherny Claude subscription cutoff analysis) is reinforced — a company spending billions on infrastructure needs revenue, and that means controlling distribution.

VC Builds AI Chief of Staff on OpenClaw — Says It's Better Than Any Human Hire

Ryan Sarver (@rsarver)

Prominent VC Ryan Sarver (ex-Twitter) built an AI chief of staff on OpenClaw that handles his fundraise, board work, and deal flow — claims it outperforms human EAs.

Worth Your Time?

Read — One-line verdict: The AI chief of staff pattern just got mainstream validation from a high-profile VC.

What's Actually Happening

Ryan Sarver — a well-known VC, former Twitter executive — built an AI agent on the OpenClaw platform that functions as his chief of staff. He's using it to manage his fundraise, board work, portfolio company support, and angel investing. He explicitly compares it favorably to human EAs and chiefs of staff he's hired in the past.

The structural shift: this is no longer a "cool demo" or a developer experiment. A sophisticated operator running a real fund with real money decided an AI agent is better than a human for this role. The use case is identical to what Every Consulting built with Claudie — always-on operational support, scheduling, communications, research.

OpenClaw appears to be positioning as the platform for building these persistent AI agents, similar to how Every Consulting uses Claude Code on a Mac mini.

What's Not Being Said
  • "Better than any human" is provocative framing. Human chiefs of staff do things AI can't: read body language in meetings, build personal relationships with LPs, handle genuine crises with judgment.
  • The comparison may be unfair — a VC's CoS role is heavily information-processing, which is exactly what AI excels at. This pattern may not generalize to all CoS roles.
  • OpenClaw's business model matters: if they're the "Heroku for AI agents," they'll face the same platform lock-in questions we have with Claude Max.
  • Sarver has strong incentives to talk this up — VCs who demonstrate AI fluency attract better deal flow from AI startups.
Where This Sits in the Bigger Picture

We're at the inflection point where "AI employee" goes from tech-insider experiment to mainstream business practice. The pattern: 1. 2024-2025: Developers build AI coding assistants for themselves 2. 2025-2026: Operators build AI agents for internal workflows (this is us, this is Sarver) 3. 2026-2027: Businesses hire "AI employees" as a standard practice

This tweet marks the transition from phase 2 to phase 3. When a prominent VC publicly says "this is better than a human hire," it gives permission to every other executive to try it.

What's Likely Coming Next
  • More "how I built my AI [role]" posts from executives. This will become a genre.
  • OpenClaw and similar platforms will raise large rounds on the back of this narrative
  • Recruiting firms and EA agencies will start feeling pressure — they'll either adapt (helping set up AI agents) or deny (claiming AI can't do it)
  • Enterprise versions of this will emerge: not one agent per person, but one agent per team or department
  • Every Consulting will get asked about this more and more

Open-Source Models Cross the Frontier Threshold

Flo Crivello (@Altimor), Om Patel (@om_patel5), Arcee.ai (@arcee_ai)

Gemma 4 (31B) and Arcee Trinity-Large-Thinking beat all frontier models except Opus 4.6 and GPT-5.2 on production evals — open-source is no longer a tier behind.

Worth Your Time?

Read. Two independent data points on the same day confirm open-source models have crossed a threshold. Not synthetic benchmarks — Lindy.ai's production evals and a major public leaderboard.

What's Actually Happening

Two things happened on April 6, 2026:

  1. Flo Crivello (Lindy.ai founder) reported that Arcee's Trinity-Large-Thinking is the first open-source model to beat Sonnet 4.6 on their production agent evals. Available under Apache 2.0 on Hugging Face.

  2. Gemma 4 (Google, 31B parameters) beat every model on a major leaderboard except Opus 4.6 and GPT-5.2. At $0.20 per run vs Opus at ~$36 (180x more), the cost gap is staggering. This model can run locally.

The structural shift: open-source models are no longer "good enough for prototyping." They're competitive on production workloads. The remaining frontier advantage (Opus 4.6, GPT-5.2) is narrow and 10-180x more expensive.

Incentive analysis: Google is giving away Gemma to commoditize the complement (cloud compute, Android ecosystem). Arcee is building an open-source business model. Both benefit from eroding the proprietary moat. Anthropic and OpenAI's remaining advantage is at the very top — and it's shrinking.

What's Not Being Said
  • "Beats on evals" and "beats on real-world production use" are different things. Flo explicitly called this out: "Now begins vibe testing." The gap between benchmark performance and reliable agentic execution is still real.
  • Nobody's talking about the implications for Claude Max pricing. If open-source catches up, the $100-200/month subscription model faces pressure.
  • The 31B parameter size means Gemma 4 fits on consumer hardware (M-series Macs with 64GB+). The "local AI" narrative just got a lot more credible.

Contrarian view: This could be a peak — model quality improvements may plateau at the 31-70B scale while the gap at the true frontier (200B+, reasoning chains, tool use) remains. The "catches up" narrative has been premature before.

Where This Sits in the Bigger Picture

This fits into the "commoditization of intelligence" arc: - 2024: Open-source was 6-12 months behind frontier - 2025: Gap narrowed to 2-3 months - 2026 (now): Gap is measured in specific capabilities, not general quality

The trajectory suggests frontier model providers will differentiate on: 1. Reliability and uptime (enterprise SLAs) 2. Agentic capabilities (tool use, multi-step reasoning) 3. Integration ecosystem (Claude Code, Copilot) 4. Safety and alignment

Raw model quality is becoming table stakes.

What's Likely Coming Next
  • Pricing pressure on API providers. Anthropic and OpenAI will face questions about why Opus costs 180x more than a model that's nearly as good.
  • Hybrid deployment models. Companies will route simple tasks to local/cheap models and escalate to frontier for complex reasoning. This is already happening informally.
  • "Model routing" becomes a standard pattern. Expect more tools like the Copilot RubberDuck (also announced today) that automatically escalate between model tiers.
  • Local-first AI becomes a real enterprise option. Finance and healthcare clients who can't send data to APIs now have a credible path.
Attention Is All You Need
  • Arcee.ai Trinity-Large-Thinking: Hugging Face (search Arcee Trinity) — Apache 2.0 weights
  • Gemma 4: Google DeepMind release — 31B parameters
  • Flo Crivello / Lindy.ai: Building production AI agents, evals reflect real agentic workloads
  • Jevons Paradox framing (also trending today): Pawel Huryn noted 67,000 open AI roles — cheaper AI = more demand, not less
What This Means for Every Consulting

Client advice implications: - Clients asking "should we use Claude/GPT?" now need a nuanced answer: "For what?" Simple tasks → open-source/local. Complex reasoning → frontier. The right answer is a routing strategy, not a single model choice. - Finance clients (Bloomberg, hedge funds): Gemma 4 running locally is a game-changer for data privacy concerns. We should proactively raise this in upcoming engagements. - Cost optimization is now a real consulting deliverable, not just "use the best model."

Training implications: - Bootcamp curriculum should include model selection and routing strategies - Mike's developer training should cover local model deployment (ollama, llama.cpp) - "Which model for which task" becomes a core competency we teach

Internal implications: - Our Claude Max dependency (flagged in memory as a platform risk) just got a partial mitigation path - We should evaluate Gemma 4 for lower-stakes Claudie tasks (X browsing, routine monitoring) - The hybrid approach (frontier for complex work, local for routine) could reduce our Max token burn

Key Shift

Meta Employees 'Tokenmaxxing' on Internal Claudeonomics Leaderboard

Jyoti Mann (@jyoti_mann1)

Meta has gamified AI usage with an internal leaderboard called 'Claudeonomics' where employees compete for token usage status — 60 trillion tokens consumed in 30 days.

Worth Your Time?

Read. This is one of the most important enterprise AI adoption signals of 2026.

One-line verdict: Meta has turned AI usage into a competitive sport — and it reveals how the largest tech companies are forcing cultural adoption through gamification, not mandates.

What's Actually Happening

Meta has built an internal leaderboard called "Claudeonomics" (named after Anthropic's Claude) that tracks AI token consumption across its 85,000+ employees. Over a recent 30-day period, total usage topped 60 trillion tokens. The top individual user averaged 281 billion tokens — which at Anthropic's public pricing would cost millions of dollars per person.

The leaderboard has gamified badges (bronze through jade) and achievement titles like "Model Connoisseur" and "Cache Wizard." Meta became the first major tech company to formally tie employee performance reviews to AI usage in 2026.

The structural reality: This isn't about productivity. This is about cultural transformation through social pressure. Meta is using the same engagement mechanics it deploys on its consumer products (status, competition, leaderboards) to reshape how its own workforce interacts with AI. The incentive structure is: use AI visibly and often, or fall behind your peers in performance reviews.

Who benefits from this narrative: Anthropic (massive implied revenue from Meta's Claude usage), Meta leadership (demonstrating "AI-native" transformation to investors), and employees who are already power users (their existing behavior is now rewarded). Who loses: Employees who do deep, focused work that doesn't involve high token throughput — and potentially Meta's bottom line, as some employees are running agents for hours on meaningless tasks just to climb the leaderboard.

Structural shift, not noise. Microsoft is doing the same thing with Copilot adoption leaderboards in Viva Insights. When the two largest enterprise tech employers both gamify AI usage metrics, it signals a new management orthodoxy forming.

What's Not Being Said

The Goodhart's Law problem is enormous. "When a measure becomes a target, it ceases to be a good measure." Employees are already gaming this — running AI agents for hours on research tasks to inflate their token counts. This is the "lines of code" metric reborn for the AI era. Duncan's analysis on his blog literally calls it "tokenmaxxing is lines-of-code thinking for the agentic era."

60 trillion tokens at public pricing would be ~$900M. Meta certainly has bulk pricing, but even at 90% discount, this is an extraordinary spend. The question nobody is asking: what's the actual ROI? Meta is measuring volume of AI usage, not value of AI usage. A developer who writes one precise prompt that solves a critical bug uses fewer tokens than someone running an agent in circles for hours — but the leaderboard rewards the latter.

The smartest contrarian take: This is a tax on thoughtfulness. The best AI users are often the most token-efficient — they know exactly what to ask, how to frame it, and when to stop. Rewarding raw consumption penalizes expertise and rewards brute force.

A historian would note: Every technology adoption wave has a "usage mandate" phase — mainframe utilization reports in the 1970s, email activity tracking in the 1990s, Slack message counts in the 2010s. The mandates always produce Goodhart effects. The real adoption happens when the tool becomes invisible — when people use it because it's genuinely faster, not because they're being watched.

Where This Sits in the Bigger Picture

AI/AGI trajectory: This confirms that enterprise AI adoption has crossed from "optional experimentation" to "mandatory performance metric." The frontier models are now good enough that companies are comfortable mandating their use company-wide. We're in the "electricity moment" — not when electricity was invented, but when factories were redesigned around electric motors instead of steam shafts. Meta is redesigning its performance culture around AI.

Labor market transformation: If AI usage becomes a standard performance metric at Meta, it will cascade to every tech company within 12-18 months, and to non-tech enterprises within 3-5 years. This creates a new axis of employee evaluation that advantages younger, AI-native workers and disadvantages experienced workers who built expertise in pre-AI workflows.

Worth adding to the radar: The emerging "AI usage as HR metric" trend. This is distinct from AI replacing jobs — it's AI usage being required to keep your job. The compliance burden falls on individual employees, not on the organization to provide useful AI tools.

What's Likely Coming Next

Most probable: Other FAANG companies will launch similar internal dashboards within Q2-Q3 2026. Google already has internal Gemini usage tracking. Amazon will likely tie AI usage to its famously rigorous performance review process.

Second-order effect: A market for "token-efficient" AI tools and workflows. If employees are rewarded for high token usage but companies want to control costs, there's tension. Smart companies will eventually shift from "tokens consumed" to "tasks completed with AI" — but that's harder to measure, so the crude token metric will persist longer than it should.

Non-obvious implication: This massively benefits Anthropic's revenue. If Meta's 85,000 employees are all incentivized to maximize Claude usage, Anthropic's enterprise revenue from Meta alone could be in the hundreds of millions annually. This creates a deep vendor lock-in — Meta's performance culture is now structurally dependent on Anthropic's API.

Watch for: (1) Employee backlash — expect anonymous posts on Blind/Reddit about being forced to "tokenmaxx" to keep their jobs. (2) The first high-profile case of an employee fired for low AI usage scores. (3) Companies starting to measure AI output quality instead of raw usage — that's when the real shift happens.

  • Gizmodo: Tech Employees Are Being Evaluated by How Fast They Burn Through LLM Tokens — broader context on the trend beyond Meta.
  • Duncan's Analysis: "Tokenmaxxing is Lines-of-Code Thinking for the Agentic Era" — the best critical take. Read the key argument: crude metrics always produce perverse incentives.
  • Microsoft Copilot Adoption Leaderboards in Viva Insights — shows this is becoming an industry pattern, not just a Meta thing.

What This Means for Every Consulting

This is directly relevant to client work:

For Natalia and the consulting practice: This is the strongest case study yet for the "AI adoption is a culture problem, not a technology problem" thesis that underpins Every Consulting's approach. When a company as sophisticated as Meta resorts to gamification and performance review integration to drive adoption, it validates that simply giving people access to AI tools isn't enough. This is exactly the problem we solve.

Specific consulting angle: Clients will start asking "how do we measure AI adoption?" — and the wrong answer is "count tokens." The right answer is what we should be developing: frameworks for measuring AI adoption by outcome quality, not volume. We should be building this into our training and discovery process now, before clients copy Meta's crude approach.

For Brooker's non-tech trainings: This is a perfect case study for explaining to non-technical leaders why "just giving everyone a ChatGPT license" doesn't work. The Meta example shows that even with unlimited access and social incentives, you get gaming and waste without proper training and workflow design — which is exactly what we provide.

For Mike's tech trainings: The token-efficiency angle is gold. Teaching developers to be precise with AI — fewer tokens, better outcomes — is the opposite of what Meta's leaderboard rewards, and it's demonstrably more valuable. Mike can position our training as "how to get 10x the value at 1/10th the tokens."

Anthropic Publishes Official Harness Design Guide for Autonomous Code Agents

Camille Roux (@CamilleRoux), source: Anthropic Engineering

Anthropic formalizes agent harness architecture: planner/generator/evaluator pattern, context resets, and separate evaluators to prevent self-congratulatory bias.

Worth Your Time?

Read. This is Anthropic publishing official engineering guidance on how to build the infrastructure layer around their models. If you build on Claude, this is your design manual.

One-line verdict: The harness layer is now a first-class concern — Anthropic is telling you the model alone isn't enough.

What's Actually Happening

Anthropic's engineering team published a detailed article on designing harnesses — the orchestration layer around autonomous code agents. The core architecture is a three-agent system inspired by GANs:

  • Planner: Expands brief prompts into full product specs
  • Generator: Implements features incrementally in sprints
  • Evaluator: Uses Playwright to test running applications like a real user, grading work against concrete criteria

The key structural insight: self-evaluation is systematically broken. When models grade their own work, they "confidently praise the work — even when, to a human observer, the quality is obviously mediocre." Separating judgment from generation makes the evaluator tunable and the generator accountable.

Context resets — clearing the window entirely with structured handoffs — are presented as essential for long-running tasks, though newer models (Opus 4.6) reduce this overhead.

The incentive structure is clear: Anthropic wants developers building sophisticated multi-agent harnesses because (a) they produce better output, (b) they use 20x more tokens ($200 vs $9 per run), and (c) they create stickier platform dependency. This is good engineering advice that also happens to be good business for Anthropic.

What's Not Being Said
  • This formalizes what power users already do. The Claude Code community has been building harnesses informally. Anthropic publishing this means they've validated these patterns at scale and want to standardize them — likely to reduce support burden from poorly-designed harnesses.
  • The 20x cost gap is buried. A full harness run costs $200 vs $9 solo. That's a real constraint for all but well-funded teams. The article doesn't dwell on this.
  • No mention of the third-party usage crackdown. Same week Anthropic publishes "build sophisticated harnesses" they're also restricting how subscription users can run claude -p with custom system prompts. Mixed signals.
Where This Sits in the Bigger Picture

This is part of a broader trend: the harness layer is becoming the new competitive moat. Harrison Chase (LangChain) flagged the "meta harness" paper the same week. The industry is converging on the idea that model intelligence is necessary but insufficient — the orchestration, evaluation, and memory infrastructure around the model is what separates working systems from demos.

This maps to the trajectory: raw model → prompt engineering → RAG → agents → harness design. Each layer adds sophistication and cost but also capability. We're firmly in the harness era.

What's Likely Coming Next
  • Anthropic will likely ship harness primitives — expect official planner/evaluator SDKs or Claude Code features that formalize these patterns
  • "Harness engineer" becomes a real job title within 6 months at companies building on Claude
  • Evaluator-as-a-service will emerge — external evaluation of agent output as a product category
  • Cost optimization for harnesses will become critical — the 20x multiplier means harness design directly impacts unit economics
Attention Is All You Need
  • Primary source: https://anthropic.com/engineering/harness-design-long-running-apps
  • Related: Harrison Chase on "meta harness" paper (@hwchase17, same week)
  • Related: Anthropic's earlier multi-agent orchestration docs
  • Key concept: GAN-inspired adversarial architecture applied to code generation (generator vs evaluator)
  • Key person: The unnamed Anthropic engineer who built this — their harness iteration methodology (reading traces, identifying judgment divergences, calibrating evaluator skepticism) is the real craft
What This Means for Every Consulting

For Nityesh / Claudie architecture: - Our current Claudie setup uses skills and scheduled jobs but lacks a formal evaluator layer. The self-congratulatory bias problem is real — when Claudie runs a scheduled job, there's no independent check on output quality. Adding a lightweight evaluator pattern to critical scheduled jobs (dashboard updates, email handling) would catch errors before they reach the team. - The "sprint contract" concept (generator and evaluator negotiate what "done" means before implementation) could improve how we define success criteria for complex tasks.

For Mike's developer training vertical: - This is curriculum gold. Enterprise developer teams need to understand that "using Claude Code" is step 1 — building effective harnesses around it is step 2. This article provides the framework for an advanced training module.

For client advisory: - When clients ask "how do we use Claude for X?" the answer is increasingly "you need a harness, not just a prompt." This article gives us a credible, Anthropic-endorsed framework to point to.

For Natalia / sales: - "Harness design consulting" could be a distinct offering — helping enterprise teams design their agent orchestration layer. This is higher-value than prompt engineering and more defensible.
